The Logical Proof of Tawhid: The Argument from Mutual Hindrance (Tamanu')
Al-Nasafi presents the definitive argument against dualism from mutual hindrance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of two gods is proved through the logical principle of mutual hindrance (tamanu').

Core Idea
Two gods cancel each other out; only one can exist.

Al-Nasafi concludes his discussion of dualist sects by presenting a crisp logical argument: if there were two creators, they would inevitably conflict. This conflict would prove either that both are created or that one is weak, and in any case cannot lead to a coherent universe. The argument is a straightforward reductio ad absurdum that every student of kalam must master.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The passage begins with a hypothetical: 'if there were two makers'. Notice how al-Nasafi builds the argument on this assumption.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Trace the logical consequences
What would happen if two makers existed? Mutual hindrance (tamanu') leads to either both being originated or one being impotent. That is impossible for a true god.
The key terms: التمانع and النقيضان.
4
Conclude the argument
Since the premise leads to impossibility, the premise is false. Therefore, there is only one Maker.
The conclusion is direct: إذا الصانع واحد وهو الله.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في وحدانية الصانع
يتحدث النسفي مثبتا أن صانع العالم لابد أن يكون واحدا؛ لأن القول بغير ذلك يؤدي إلى الوقوع في المحال، ويدخل في مناقشة طويلة مع المعتزلة، ونراه يتهم أبا هاشم الجبائي بالإلحاد، لأن الأخير قال بأن وحدانية الصانع لا تعرف بالعقل وإنما بالسمع.
يقول أبو المعين: ولعل مراد هذا الملحد إبطال الوحدانية، وإفساد القول بالرسالة، وهذا منه قمة القسوة على أبي هاشم، ولعل ما يخفف وقع هذا الكلام علينا أننا رأينا المعتزلة وعلى رأسهم القاضي عبد الجبار يشنون نفس الحملة ويتهمون الأشاعرة والماتريدية بأنهم المجبرة والقدرية.
وبعد مناقشته للمعتزلة يبدأ في حديث عن المجوس فيعرفنا بهم، وبنشأة فكرة الإثنينية عندهم، ثم يورد شبههم، ويرد عليها، مبينا أن للشرور في العالم فوائد، وهذا يبطل قول المجوس بوجود يزدان إله الخير، وأهر من إله الشر.
ثم يعرفنا بالثنوية، وأقسامهم المانوية، والديصانية، والمرقيونية، ويناقش فكرهم ليبطله، ويطيل في المناقشة ومع الإطالة يقول : إن هذا الكلام تبرع منه إذ لا حاجة بنا إلى شيء من هذا، لأنه لو كان هناك صانعان لوجد التمانع، والتمانع يدل على حدوثهما ، أو حدوث العاجز منهما، لأنه لو حصل ما يريدانه في الشيء لاجتمع النقيضان وهذا محال إذا الصانع واحد وهو الله.
Discussion on the Oneness of the Maker. Al-Nasafi speaks, affirming that the Maker of the world must be one, because saying otherwise leads to absurdity. He enters into a long discussion with the Mu'tazila, and we see him accusing Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i of heresy, because the latter said that the oneness of the Maker is known not by reason but by revelation. Abu al-Mu'in says: Perhaps the intention of this heretic is to nullify oneness and corrupt the assertion of the message. This from him is extreme harshness toward Abu Hashim. Perhaps what alleviates the impact of this statement on us is that we have seen the Mu'tazila, led by al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar, launching the same campaign and accusing the Ash'aris and Maturidis of being Jabriyya (predestinarians) and Qadariyya (free-will advocates). After his discussion with the Mu'tazila, he begins to talk about the Magians, acquainting us with them and the origin of the idea of dualism among them. Then he presents their doubts and refutes them, showing that evils in the world have benefits, and this refutes the Magians' belief in Yazdan, the god of good, and Ahriman, the god of evil. Then he acquaints us with the dualists (Thaniwiyya) and their divisions: Manichaeans, Daysaniyya, and Marqiyuniyya. He discusses their thought to refute it, and he prolongs the discussion, and with the prolongation he says: This speech is a gratuitous addition, for we have no need of any of this, because if there were two makers, there would be mutual hindrance (tamanu'), and mutual hindrance indicates their origination, or the origination of the impotent one, because if both intend something in a thing, two contradictories would combine, and that is impossible. So the Maker is one, and He is Allah.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents al-Nasafi's argument for the oneness of the creator. It begins by stating that the Maker must be one, as multiplicity leads to absurdity (muhal). Al-Nasafi then engages with the Mu'tazila, specifically criticizing Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i for claiming that divine oneness is known through revelation rather than reason, which al-Nasafi considers heretical. The author of the summary (likely Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi) notes that the Mu'tazila similarly attack Ash'aris and Maturidis. Next, al-Nasafi discusses the Magians (Majus) and their dualistic idea of two gods (Yazdan and Ahriman), refuting it by arguing that evils have benefits. He then discusses other dualist sects (Manichaeans, Daysaniyya, Marqiyuniyya). Finally, he concludes that if there were two creators, there would be mutual hindrance (tamanu'), which would prove either that both are originated or that one is impotent. If both will the same thing, it would require the combination of contradictories, which is impossible. Therefore, the creator must be one, and He is Allah.

Key Terms
التمانس (tamanu')
النقيضان (contradictories)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from mutual hindrance requires that two gods actually exist to be refuted.
Correction: It is a reductio: we assume their existence and deduce an impossibility, proving they cannot exist.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's argument, what follows from the existence of two makers?
A) They would cooperate to create the world.
B) There would be mutual hindrance, leading to impossibility.
C) One would be good and the other evil.
D) They would be unknowable.

Key Takeaway
The logical impossibility of two creators is proven by the unavoidable mutual hindrance they would cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have now seen that belief in more than one God is logically absurd. But do you still act as if there are other forces controlling your life – like people, money, or your own desires? What is one specific attachment you can cut this week, to prove you truly believe Allah alone is the Creator and Disposer of all affairs?
?
The proof from mutual hindrance shows that any rival to Allah would lead to contradiction and chaos. Look at your daily worries: do you ever fear that something besides Allah can harm or benefit you independently? Repent of that hidden shirk and commit to reciting say, 'Hasbi Allahu la ilaha illa huwa' (Allah suffices me, there is no god but Him) whenever that fear arises.
?
If the existence of two gods is impossible, then your heart's division between love of Allah and love of the world is also a logical chaos. When will you stop trying to serve two masters and fully surrender your heart to the One who alone deserves your entire trust and devotion?

Reason vs. Revelation in Knowing Tawhid: Al-Nasafi's Critique of Abu Hashim
Al-Nasafi criticizes Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i for denying the rational knowability of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi insists that the oneness of God must be known through reason, not revelation alone, and why he accuses Abu Hashim of heresy.

Core Idea
Tawhid is a rational necessity, not just a revealed truth.

Before presenting his own arguments, al-Nasafi engages with the Mu'tazila, particularly Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, who claimed that divine oneness is only known through revelation. Al-Nasafi considers this view heretical because it undermines the rational foundation of belief. This passage highlights the critical debate within Islamic theology about the role of reason in establishing fundamental doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the strong language used against Abu Hashim.
Al-Nasafi calls him a heretic (ملحد).
2
Identify the two positions
Al-Nasafi asserts that oneness is known by reason; Abu Hashim says it is known only by revelation. Understand each position.
3
Assess the accusation
Why does al-Nasafi think Abu Hashim's view leads to nullifying oneness? Consider the consequences.
If reason cannot prove tawhid, then revelation could be corrupted.
4
Note the counter-accusation
The Mu'tazila accused Ash'aris and Maturidis of being Jabriyya and Qadariyya. This shows the polemical context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في وحدانية الصانع
يتحدث النسفي مثبتا أن صانع العالم لابد أن يكون واحدا؛ لأن القول بغير ذلك يؤدي إلى الوقوع في المحال، ويدخل في مناقشة طويلة مع المعتزلة، ونراه يتهم أبا هاشم الجبائي بالإلحاد، لأن الأخير قال بأن وحدانية الصانع لا تعرف بالعقل وإنما بالسمع.

He enters into a long discussion with the Mu'tazila, and we see him accusing Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i of heresy, because the latter said that the oneness of the Maker is known not by reason but by revelation. Key Terms
وحدانية الصانع (tawhid)
العقل (reason)
السمع (revelation)
الإلحاد (heresy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawhid can only be known through revelation, and reason has no role in proving it.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that tawhid must be known by reason, otherwise the entire edifice of faith lacks rational grounding and can be easily corrupted.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafi accuse Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i of heresy?
A) Because Abu Hashim denied the oneness of God.
B) Because Abu Hashim said that oneness is known only through revelation, not reason.
C) Because Abu Hashim believed in two gods.
D) Because Abu Hashim claimed reason alone cannot know anything about God.

Key Takeaway
For al-Nasafi, denying the rational necessity of tawhid is akin to heresy because it removes the most fundamental proof of God's oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Nasafi did not mince words; he called Abu Hashim a heretic. Is there a belief about Allah that you hold so firmly that you would consider its denial a threat to the entire faith? What would you say to someone who disagrees with you on that point?
?
The Mu'tazila also attacked their opponents. When you engage in theological disagreements, do you seek to understand the other's reasoning or quickly label them misguided? How does this passage expose your own tendency toward partisanship?
?
Abu Hashim's claim that tawhid is known only by revelation might seem cautious to some. But al-Nasafi saw it as a dangerous reduction. What 'safe' opinions have you held that, upon reflection, could undermine a fundamental truth? Will you reconsider them today?

Refuting Magian Dualism: The Problem of Evil and Its Benefits
Al-Nasafi discusses Magian dualism and refutes it by showing that evil has benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the Magian belief in two gods (good and evil) by arguing that evil serves a purpose and has benefits.

Core Idea
Evil in the world has benefits, disproving the need for a separate evil god.

After dealing with the Mu'tazila, al-Nasafi turns to the Magians (Majus), who believe in two eternal principles: Yazdan (good) and Ahriman (evil). Their strongest argument is the existence of evil in the world. Al-Nasafi responds by showing that evils have hidden benefits, thus undercutting the need for a second god of evil. This lesson examines his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that al-Nasafi first introduces the Magians and their belief.
2
Understand the Magian argument
The Magians see evil as proof of an evil creator alongside a good creator.
They point to natural disasters, disease, etc.
3
Identify al-Nasafi's response
Al-Nasafi counters that evils have benefits (فوائد) in the larger scheme of things. This undermines the dualist explanation.
He does not deny evil but reinterprets it.
4
Consider the implication
If evil can be explained within a monotheistic framework, dualism is unnecessary.
This leads to the final argument of tamanu'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في وحدانية الصانع
يتحدث النسفي مثبتا أن صانع العالم لابد أن يكون واحدا؛ لأن القول بغير ذلك يؤدي إلى الوقوع في المحال، ويدخل في مناقشة طويلة مع المعتزلة، ونراه يتهم أبا هاشم الجبائي بالإلحاد، لأن الأخير قال بأن وحدانية الصانع لا تعرف بالعقل وإنما بالسمع.
يقول أبو المعين: ولعل مراد هذا الملحد إبطال الوحدانية، وإفساد القول بالرسالة، وهذا منه قمة القسوة على أبي هاشم، ولعل ما يخفف وقع هذا الكلام علينا أننا رأينا المعتزلة وعلى رأسهم القاضي عبد الجبار يشنون نفس الحملة ويتهمون الأشاعرة والماتريدية بأنهم المجبرة والقدرية.
وبعد مناقشته للمعتزلة يبدأ في حديث عن المجوس فيعرفنا بهم، وبنشأة فكرة الإثنينية عندهم، ثم يورد شبههم، ويرد عليها، مبينا أن للشرور في العالم فوائد، وهذا يبطل قول المجوس بوجود يزدان إله الخير، وأهر من إله الشر.
ثم يعرفنا بالثنوية، وأقسامهم المانوية، والديصانية، والمرقيونية، ويناقش فكرهم ليبطله، ويطيل في المناقشة ومع الإطالة يقول : إن هذا الكلام تبرع منه إذ لا حاجة بنا إلى شيء من هذا، لأنه لو كان هناك صانعان لوجد التمانع، والتمانع يدل على حدوثهما ، أو حدوث العاجز منهما، لأنه لو حصل ما يريدانه في الشيء لاجتمع النقيضان وهذا محال إذا الصانع واحد وهو الله.
Discussion on the Oneness of the Maker. Al-Nasafi speaks, affirming that the Maker of the world must be one, because saying otherwise leads to absurdity. He enters into a long discussion with the Mu'tazila, and we see him accusing Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i of heresy, because the latter said that the oneness of the Maker is known not by reason but by revelation. Commentary
This passage presents al-Nasafi's argument for the oneness of the creator. It begins by stating that the Maker must be one, as multiplicity leads to absurdity (muhal). Al-Nasafi then engages with the Mu'tazila, specifically criticizing Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i for claiming that divine oneness is known through revelation rather than reason, which al-Nasafi considers heretical. The author of the summary (likely Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi) notes that the Mu'tazila similarly attack Ash'aris and Maturidis. Next, al-Nasafi discusses the Magians (Majus) and their dualistic idea of two gods (Yazdan and Ahriman), refuting it by arguing that evils have benefits. He then discusses other dualist sects (Manichaeans, Daysaniyya, Marqiyuniyya). Finally, he concludes that if there were two creators, there would be mutual hindrance (tamanu'), which would prove either that both are originated or that one is impotent. If both will the same thing, it would require the combination of contradictories, which is impossible. Therefore, the creator must be one, and He is Allah.

Key Terms
المجوس (Magians)
الإثنينية (dualism)
يزدان (Yazdan)
أهر من (Ahriman)
فوائد (benefits)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of evil proves that there must be two separate creators—one good and one evil.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that evil has benefits and can be explained within a single-creator framework, thus dualism is unnecessary.

Checkpoint
How does al-Nasafi refute the Magian belief in two gods?
A) By denying that evil exists.
B) By arguing that evils have benefits.
C) By saying that the Magians misunderstood their own scriptures.
D) By claiming that reason cannot know about evil.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's refutation of Magian dualism rests on the claim that apparent evils serve beneficial purposes, thus no second creator is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship that seems purely evil, do you ever doubt the absolute oneness of Allah, as if the evil comes from a source other than Him? How does al-Nasafi's claim that all evils have hidden benefits challenge that doubt? Will you commit to searching for that benefit in your current trial before complaining?
?
You believe in one God intellectually, but when something bad happens, do you blame 'bad luck', 'fate', or 'people' as if they were a second power? Examine your heart: Are you truly attributing both good and evil to Allah, trusting that even the evil has a purpose? Starting today, practice saying 'This is from Allah, and He does all things well.'
?
Think of a specific difficulty you have faced or are facing. Without glossing over the pain, can you identify at least one benefit that came from it—perhaps patience, trust, or a lesson? If you cannot see it, does al-Nasafi's argument give you the confidence to ask Allah to show you the benefit? Pray tonight for the wisdom to see the fawāʾid in your life.

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
The Creator Is Not an Accident
Al-Nasafi proves the Creator is not an accident using definition and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Creator cannot be an accident based on the definition of accident and the attributes of self-subsistence.

Core Idea
Accidents are fleeting; the Creator is eternal and self-subsistent.

This lesson examines a foundational argument in Islamic theology: the Creator cannot be an accident. Al-Nasafi provides two clear reasons based on the nature of accidents and the unique attributes of the Creator. Let's explore these reasons step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the author's direct meaning.
2
Understand the definition of accident
Identify how the passage defines an accident (ʿaraḍ): something that cannot remain (i.e., it is temporary).
This definition is key to the whole argument.
3
Contrast accident with Creator's eternity
Notice that the Creator is described as 'remaining without end' (bāqin lā nihāya). Since accidents cannot remain, the Creator cannot be an accident.
4
Contrast accident's dependence with Creator's self-subsistence
Accidents do not subsist by themselves; they need a substance. The Creator subsists by Himself and nothing else subsists by Him.
This second argument reinforces the first.
5
Conclude the teaching point
Synthesize both arguments: the Creator is eternal and self-subsistent, so He is not an accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن العرض ما يستحيل بقاؤه إذا الباري لا يكون عرضا، لأنه باق بلا نهاية، كما أن العرض لا يقوم بذاته، والباري يقوم بذاته، ولا يقوم به غيره.
That the accident is that which cannot remain, so the Creator is not an accident, because He remains without end, and also the accident does not subsist by itself, while the Creator subsists by Himself, and nothing else subsists by Him.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument excluding accidents from God. First, it defines an accident (ʿaraḍ) as something that cannot remain (i.e., it is fleeting and does not persist for two consecutive moments). The Creator, in contrast, is eternal and persists without end. Therefore, He cannot be an accident. Secondly, accidents are not self-subsistent (qāʾim bi-dhātihi); they inhere in substances. The Creator is self-subsistent and does not depend on anything else. Additionally, He is not a substrate for other attributes in the same way accidents inhere. Hence, the Creator is not an accident.

Key Terms
عرض
بقاء
قائم بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Creator might be an accident because His attributes are not self-subsistent.
Correction: Accidents are temporary and depend on substances, whereas the Creator is eternal and self-subsistent.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of an accident (ʿaraḍ) given in this passage?
A) Something that has three dimensions
B) Something that is extended in space
C) Something that cannot remain
D) Something that is composed

Key Takeaway
The Creator is not an accident because accidents are fleeting and dependent, while the Creator is eternal and self-subsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Creator is not an accident—fleeting and dependent—why do you build your life on fleeting pleasures and depend on creation instead of the Self-Subsistent One? What is one specific dependency you need to confess and realign today?
?
You affirm that God alone is eternal and self-subsistent. Examine your heart: where have you placed your ultimate trust in something that will not last? What is one practical step you can take this week to shift that trust back to Him?
?
The accident cannot remain; everything you love except God will perish. Have you prepared your soul for that reality? As you reflect on this lesson, what is one attachment—a relationship, a possession, or a status—that you need to relativize or release because it is, in essence, an 'accident' in your life?

Divine Knowledge vs. Created Knowledge
Nasafi proves the dissimilarity of divine and created knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge is fundamentally different from human knowledge according to Nasafi, and how this follows from the general principle of divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not like our knowledge—it is neither acquired nor necessary, but eternal.

Nasafi continues to build the case for divine transcendence. Having argued that God cannot be compared to creatures in essence, he now extends that to their attributes, specifically knowledge. The passage draws a sharp line: human knowledge falls into two categories, but God's knowledge belongs to neither. This lesson unpacks that line of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument is condensed and follows a logical sequence.
2
Trace the logical argument
Identify the conditional premise (no similarity between God and creatures) and see how Nasafi deduces the same for knowledge. Then find the specific reasons why the knowledge cannot be similar.
The premise is stated at the start; the conclusion comes immediately.
3
Contrast the two descriptions
Compare the attributes of human knowledge (kasbi/daruri, hadith) with divine knowledge (neither, eternal, imperishable). Write down the differences in your own words.
This contrast is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت أنه لا مماثلة بين الله، وبين المخلوقين ثبت أنه لا مماثلة بين علم الله، وبين علم المخلوقين، لأن علم المخلوقين، إما كسبي وإما ضروري، وعلم الله لا يوصف بالاكتساب أو الضرورة، بل هو علم لا يفنى، كذلك فإن علم المخلوقين حادث وعلم الله قديم.
And if it is established that there is no similarity between God and the creatures, then it is established that there is no similarity between God's knowledge and the knowledge of the creatures. For the knowledge of the creatures is either acquired (kasbī) or necessary (ḍarūrī), and God's knowledge is not described as acquired or necessary; rather it is knowledge that does not perish. Likewise, the knowledge of the creatures is contingent (ḥādith) and God's knowledge is eternal (qadīm).
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the dissimilarity between divine and human knowledge. The author builds on the previously established principle that there is no likeness between God and creatures. From this, he deduces that their respective knowledges also cannot be similar. He then explains the difference: human knowledge is either acquired (kasbī) through effort or necessary (ḍarūrī) like innate knowledge, whereas God's knowledge is neither acquired nor necessary; it is eternal and imperishable. Additionally, created knowledge is contingent (ḥādith) while God's knowledge is eternal (qadīm).

Key Terms
علم (knowledge)
مماثلة (similarity)
كسبي (acquired)
ضروري (necessary)
حادث (contingent)
قديم (eternal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since humans have knowledge, God's knowledge is similar but greater in scale or perfection.
Correction: The passage insists it is entirely different in nature: human knowledge is either acquired or necessary, and it is contingent, whereas divine knowledge is neither acquired nor necessary, and is eternal and imperishable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is God's knowledge described?
A) Acquired and necessary
B) Contingent
C) Neither acquired nor necessary
D) Both acquired and necessary

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is entirely different from human knowledge: it is neither acquired nor necessary, but eternal and imperishable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking of God's knowledge as being like a supercomputer or a perfect encyclopedia—just more of what you already have? How does this lesson expose that as a subtle form of shirk (associating partners)?
?
If God's knowledge is neither acquired nor necessary, then your own knowledge—whether learned or intuitive—can never grasp Him fully. Does that humble you, or frustrate you? What would change in your prayer (du'a) if you truly believed your knowledge is utterly unlike His?
?
You rely on your acquired knowledge every day—your education, your reasoning, your opinions. But the passage says God's knowledge is not like that. How much of your confidence in matters of faith is actually resting on your own kasbī or ḍarūrī knowledge rather than on submission to God's eternal knowledge? Are you willing to let go of that confidence?

Rational Proof for God's Attributes and Necessity of Life
Nasafi proves that originated things necessitate a Maker with power, knowledge, and life, refuting those who deny life as condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the existence of originated things to prove that their Maker must possess power, knowledge, and life, and why life is a necessary condition for these attributes.

Core Idea
Only a living, powerful, knowing being can create a perfect world.

This passage presents a foundational rational argument for God's attributes. Nasafi observes that the existence of originated things demands a Maker who possesses perfection. He then focuses on the specific attributes of power and knowledge, and argues that they cannot exist without life. The passage also directly refutes those who deny life as a condition for these attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's flow guide you before interpreting.
2
Identify the main premises
Find the premises: (1) Everything around indicates a perfect Maker. (2) Originated things require power and knowledge. (3) Life is a condition for power and knowledge.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the argument: originated things → Maker must have power and knowledge → such attributes require life → so Maker must be living.
4
Understand the refutation
Note that this argument refutes those who deny life as a condition for knowledge and power, specifically Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi and the Karramiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتحدث النسفي في إثبات صفات الله، فينظر في الوجود، فيرى أن كل من حوله، وما حوله يشير إلى وجود صانع مدبر متصف بكل صفات الكمال، لأنه إذا لم يتصف بها، لاتصف بضدها، وضدها نقائص ، والناقص لا يوجد الكامل. ويقول: إن المحدثات توجب اتصاف محدثها بالقدرة والعلم إذ لا فعل يأتي بدون القدرة، ولا إحكام يكون بدون العلم، وكما لا يتصور وجود شيء بدون القدرة، والعلم، لا يتصور وجوده إلا من حي بل إن الحياة شرط في ثبوت العلم والقدرة. إذ الموت والجمادية مضادان للعلم والقدرة. وبهذا الكلام يكون الرد على أبي الحسين الصالحي والكرامية اللذين يقولان: إن الحياة ليست بشرط في ثبوت العلم.
Nasafi speaks about establishing God's attributes. He contemplates existence, and sees that everyone and everything around him indicates the existence of a Maker, a Manager, described with all attributes of perfection. Because if He were not described with them, He would be described with their opposites, and their opposites are deficiencies, and the deficient cannot produce the perfect. And he says: The originated things necessitate that their originator be described with power and knowledge, for no action comes without power, and no precision occurs without knowledge. And just as the existence of something cannot be conceived without power and knowledge, it cannot be conceived except from a living being. Rather, life is a condition for the establishment of knowledge and power. Indeed, death and inanimateness are opposite to knowledge and power. And by this speech is the refutation of Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi and the Karramiyya, who say that life is not a condition for the establishment of knowledge.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for God's attributes. Nasafi begins by observing that everything around him points to a Maker described with all perfections. The logic is: if the Maker were not perfect, He would have deficiencies, and a deficient being cannot produce a perfect world. Then he argues specifically that originated things (muhdathat) require their originator to have power and knowledge, because actions require power and precision requires knowledge. Furthermore, nothing possessing power and knowledge can exist except that it is living. Life is a necessary condition (shart) for the establishment of knowledge and power. Death and inanimateness are contrary to knowledge and power. Having established this, Nasafi states that this reasoning refutes Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi and the Karramiyya, who denied that life is a condition for knowledge. Thus, the passage is both a constructive proof of God's attributes and a refutation of a specific opposing view.

Key Terms
محدثات
قدرة
علم
حياة
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that life is not a necessary condition for knowledge and power in God.
Correction: Nasafi argues that power and knowledge cannot exist without life, as death and inanimateness are opposite to these attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the relationship between life, knowledge, and power?
A) Life is independent of knowledge and power
B) Life is a condition for knowledge and power
C) Knowledge and power can exist without life
D) Life is a result of knowledge and power

Key Takeaway
Originated things prove that their Maker must be living, powerful, and knowing, as life is a necessary condition for these attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the perfection of creation points to a perfect Maker, what does the disorder and imperfection in your own life reveal about your relationship with that Maker?
?
Nasafi argues that death and inanimateness cannot produce knowledge or power. Where in your life have you relied on 'dead' things (status, wealth, habits) for life and wisdom?
?
The Karramiyya denied life as a condition for knowledge. Are there any areas of your belief where you try to separate God's life from His attributes, making Him merely a force rather than a living Lord?

Pre-eternity of God's Knowledge and Knowledge of the Non-Existent
Nasafi refutes Jahm and Zurariyya on God's knowledge being created, and affirms God's knowledge includes the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Nasafi refutes the claim that God's knowledge was created, affirms the pre-eternity of God's attributes, and asserts that God's knowledge encompasses the non-existent.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is both pre-eternal and all-encompassing, including the non-existent.

This passage addresses two key points of anti-orthodox theology: the claim that God's knowledge was created in time, and the claim that God cannot know what does not exist. By refuting these errors, Nasafi upholds the perfection of God's knowledge as eternal and unlimited.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi first mentions the erroneous views, then refutes them, and finally makes a positive assertion.
2
Identify the views being refuted
Identify the views of Jahm and Zurariyya. What did they claim about God's knowledge? Why is this problematic?
Jahm said God was not knowledgeable until He created knowledge for Himself.
3
Understand Nasafi's refutation method
Nasafi says he discusses their views to invalidate them. He then establishes the pre-eternity of God's knowledge, power, and life.
This shows that divine attributes are eternal, not acquired.
4
Examine the second point: knowledge of the non-existent
Nasafi expands on God's knowledge: He knew His essence, attributes, and everything beyond that. He explicitly permits that the non-existent falls under God's knowledge.
This counters those who restrict knowledge to existent things.
5
Reflect on the theological importance
Consider why pre-eternal knowledge and knowledge of the non-existent are necessary for divine omniscience. How do these affirmations protect tawhid?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر رأي جهم - أن الله لم يكن في الأزل عالما، حتى خلق لنفسه علما، وكذا رأي الزرارية الذي يشبه رأي جهم، ويناقش آراءهما ليبطلها، ويثبت أزلية علم الله وقدرته، وحياته. أما عن علمه بما وراء ذاته في الأزل فيقول : إنه كان عالما بذاته تعالى وصفاته ومما وراء ذلك، وجوز دخول المعدوم تحت علم الله.
And he mentions the view of Jahm — that God was not knowledgeable in pre-eternity, until He created knowledge for Himself — and likewise the view of the Zurariyya which resembles Jahm's view. And he discusses their views to invalidate them, and he establishes the pre-eternity of God's knowledge, power, and life. As for His knowledge of that which is beyond His essence in pre-eternity, he says: He was knowledgeable of His essence, exalted be He, and His attributes, and of that which is beyond that, and he permitted the non-existent to fall under God's knowledge.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and positive assertion. Nasafi mentions the deviant view of Jahm ibn Safwan, who claimed that God was not knowledgeable in pre-eternity (azal) but later created knowledge for Himself. The Zurariyya held a similar view. Nasafi states that he discusses their views in order to invalidate them and to establish the pre-eternity of God's knowledge, power, and life. Then he moves to a further point: concerning God's knowledge of things other than His essence in pre-eternity. He affirms that God was knowledgeable of His own essence, His attributes, and of what is beyond that. He also explicitly permitted (jawwaza) that the non-existent (al-ma'dum) falls under God's knowledge. This is a direct refutation of those like Hisham ibn al-Hakam who denied knowledge of the non-existent, which is addressed in the next lesson.

Key Terms
الأزل
علم قديم
المعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God cannot know what does not exist, because knowledge requires an object.
Correction: Nasafi affirms that God's knowledge encompasses the non-existent, meaning He knows all possibilities even if they never come into existence. This is necessary for perfect foreknowledge.

Checkpoint

A) God created knowledge for Himself in pre-eternity.
B) God was not knowledgeable until He acquired knowledge.
C) God was knowledgeable of His essence, attributes, and the non-existent.
D) God's knowledge only covers existing things.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is pre-eternal and encompasses all things, including the non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Nasafi's Dilemma on Knowledge of the Non-Existent
Nasafi refutes Hisham's denial of knowledge of the non-existent via a dilemma about future reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses a logical dilemma to refute the claim that God cannot know the non-existent.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of the future proves He knows the non-existent.

In this passage, Nasafi confronts a sectarian denial of God's knowledge of anything beyond His essence. He constructs a brilliant dilemma argument based on the Qur'anic reports of future events. By forcing the opponent into a series of choices, he demonstrates that their position leads to either disbelief, absurdity, or self-contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Hisham ibn 'Amr argued that God does not know anything beyond His own essence, because knowledge cannot attach to non-existent things (al-ma'dum).
This is a key premise of their position.
2
Follow Nasafi's dilemma
Nasafi first gives a general refutation: denying knowledge of the non-existent implies that precise actions exist without knowledge from their doer, which is impossible. Then he presents a dilemma based on the Quranic reports of future events.
The dilemma has two main branches: whether God informed about the future or not.
3
Recognize the final refutation
The opponent is forced to either admit that God knew the non-existent when He informed about future events, or face absurd consequences like claiming resurrection occurred immediately after death.
Thus their original claim is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال هشام بن الحكم وهشام بن عمرو : لم يكن الله عالما بما وراء ذاته لأن تعلق العلم بالمعدوم محال ويرد النسفي على هذه الآراء بإبطالها قائلا: من لم يجوز العلم بالمعدوم جعل وجود الأفعال المحكمة لا عن علم لفاعلها بها، وهو محال ويسأل قائلا: هل أخبر الله عما يكون في المستقبل؟ فإن قالوا: لا . كفروا لإنكارهم نصوص القرآن. وإن قالوا نعم . سئلوا هل أخبر وهو عالم أم وهو غير عالم؟ فإن قالوا بالثانية لا يعرف صحة خبره، وبالتالي يؤدي هذا إلى إنكار ما ورد في القرآن وإن قالوا بالأولى، قيل لهم : أكان ما أخبر عنه موجودا أم كان معدوما؟ فإن قالوا موجودا فقد ادعوا وجود البعث بعد الموت مباشرة وهذه سفسطة. وإن قالوا: كان معدوما ، أبطلوا مذهبهم، واعترفوا بكون المعدوم معلوما. وبعد إقامة الدلالة العقلية يفند كلام الخصوم، ويبين أن فهمهم للقرآن قاصر .
And Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Hisham ibn 'Amr said: God was not knowledgeable of that which is beyond His essence, because the attachment of knowledge to the non-existent is impossible. And Nasafi refutes these views by invalidating them, saying: Whoever does not permit knowledge of the non-existent has made the existence of precise actions without knowledge from their doer, and that is impossible. And he asks, saying: Did God inform about what will happen in the future? If they say: No, they disbelieve by denying the texts of the Quran. And if they say: Yes, they are asked: Did He inform while being knowledgeable or while not being knowledgeable? If they say the second, the veracity of His report is not known, and this leads to denial of what is in the Quran. And if they say the first, they are told: Was what He informed about existent or non-existent? If they say existent, they have claimed the existence of resurrection immediately after death, and this is sophistry. And if they say: It was non-existent, they invalidate their own doctrine, and they admit that the non-existent is known. And after establishing the rational evidence, he dismantles the speech of the opponents and shows that their understanding of the Quran is deficient.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. Nasafi presents the view of Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Hisham ibn 'Amr who denied that God knows anything other than His essence because knowledge cannot attach to the non-existent. Nasafi refutes this in two ways. First, he gives a summary general refutation: denying knowledge of the non-existent leads to the absurd conclusion that precise actions (like those in the world) exist without the doer's knowledge. Then he presents a powerful dilemma argument. He asks his opponents: Did God inform about future events in the Quran? If they say no, they deny explicit Quranic verses and thus disbelieve. If they say yes, he asks: Was He knowing or not knowing when He informed? If not knowing, we cannot trust His report, which leads to denial of the Quran. If knowing, then he asks: Was the thing He informed about existent or non-existent at that time? If they say existent, that would mean resurrection occurred immediately after death, which is false. If they say non-existent, they admit that God knew the non-existent, which contradicts their original claim. Thus their position is self-defeating. Nasafi concludes that after establishing rational evidence, he dismantles their arguments and shows their deficient understanding of the Quran.

Key Terms
المعدوم
تعلق العلم
أخبر
مستقبل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that knowledge must be of something that exists at the time of knowing.
Correction: Nasafi shows that God's knowledge of future events involves knowing non-existent things, which is necessary for His foreknowledge and prophecies.

Checkpoint
In Nasafi's dilemma, what conclusion does he force on the opponent who says God informed about future events while knowing?
A) They must say the future events were existent
B) They must say God cannot know the future
C) They must admit that the non-existent is known
D) They must deny Quranic texts

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's dilemma proves that denying God's knowledge of the non-existent leads to self-contradiction or absurdity, affirming that God knows all things, including the non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God knows every future event, even your next thought, how does that change the way you prepare for prayer or repentance? Will you begin each act of worship with the awareness that He already knows your sincerity?
?
Nasafi argues that denying God's knowledge of the non-existent leads to absurdities. Are there areas of your life where you act as if God doesn't know your future choices—perhaps in secret sins or procrastination? What would change if you fully internalized that He does know, and that nothing escapes His knowledge?
?
The Hishams claimed God only knows His essence. How often do you reduce God's knowledge to what is comfortable for your limited understanding? In what specific situation today can you acknowledge that God knows what you cannot even imagine, and submit to that truth in your heart?

Proof of God's Necessary Attributes
Nasafi proves God must have power, knowledge, and life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that contingent things necessitate their Creator to possess power, knowledge, and life.

Core Idea
Contingent things point to a Creator with power, knowledge, and life.

Nasafi begins by establishing a fundamental principle: if God lacked essential attributes, He would be described by their opposites, which are deficiencies. Since the deficient cannot produce the perfect, the existence of contingent things proves that their Originator must have power, knowledge, and life. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the chain of reasoning
The passage begins with a principle about opposites, then applies it to contingent things, then concludes that the Creator must have power, knowledge, and life. Trace each step.
3
Understand why life is a condition
Nasafi argues that life is a condition for power and knowledge, because their opposites (death and inanimateness) are deficiencies. Grasp this logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يتصف بها، لاتصف بضدها، وضدها نقائص ، والناقص لا يوجد الكامل. ويقول: إن المحدثات توجب اتصاف محدثها بالقدرة والعلم إذ لا فعل يأتي بدون القدرة، ولا إحكام يكون بدون العلم، وكما لا يتصور وجود شيء بدون القدرة، والعلم، لا يتصور وجوده إلا من حي بل إن الحياة شرط في ثبوت العلم والقدرة. إذ الموت والجمادية مضادان للعلم والقدرة. وبهذا الكلام يكون الرد على أبي الحسين الصالحي والكرامية اللذين يقولان: إن الحياة ليست بشرط في ثبوت .العلم. ويذكر رأي جهم - أن الله لم يكن في الأزل عالما، حتى خلق لنفسه ،علما، وكذا رأي الزرارية الذي يشبه رأي جهم، ويناقش آراءهما ليبطلها، ويثبت أزلية علم الله وقدرته، وحياته.
He does not possess [the attributes] without being described by their opposite, and their opposite are deficiencies, and the deficient does not produce the perfect. He says: contingent things necessitate that their originator be described with power and knowledge, for no action occurs without power, and no perfect work occurs without knowledge. And just as it is inconceivable for something to exist without power and knowledge, it is inconceivable for it to exist except from a living being; indeed life is a condition for the affirmation of knowledge and power. For death and inanimateness are opposites to knowledge and power. This speech constitutes a refutation of Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi and the Karramiyya who say: life is not a condition for the affirmation of knowledge. And he mentions the opinion of Jahm — that God was not knowing in pre-eternity until He created for Himself knowledge, and likewise the opinion of al-Zarariyya which resembles the opinion of Jahm, and he discusses their opinions to invalidate them, and affirms the eternity of God's knowledge, power, and life.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage begins with a proof based on the principle of necessity: if a being lacks attributes, it would be described by their opposites, which are deficiencies, and a deficient being cannot produce perfect things. Therefore, contingent things (muhdathat) require that their originator possess power and knowledge, because no action occurs without power and no perfect work without knowledge. Moreover, power and knowledge require life as a condition, since death and inanimateness are opposite to them. Hence the Creator must be living. Nasafi then uses this proof to refute Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi and the Karramiyya who claimed that life is not a condition for knowledge. He also refutes Jahm and al-Zarariyya who denied that God was knowing in pre-eternity, arguing that He created knowledge for Himself later. Through this, Nasafi establishes the eternity of God's knowledge, power, and life.

Key Terms
المحدثات
القدرة
العلم
الحياة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the attributes are arbitrarily chosen by theologians, but the argument shows they are logically necessary from the existence of contingent things.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that power, knowledge, and life are not arbitrary but are required by reason.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi argue that God must have life?
A) Because contingent things require power and knowledge, and life is a condition for them.
B) Because the Quran says God is living.
C) Because life is a good attribute.
D) Because death is bad.

Key Takeaway
The existence of contingent things logically proves that God has power, knowledge, and life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the contingent universe proves that its Creator must be powerful, knowledgeable, and living, then consider: in what area of your life are you acting as if you are the creator, as if your power and knowledge are sufficient? How does this proof expose that illusion and call you to rely on the true Creator?
?
The passage argues that a deficient being cannot produce a perfect work. Look at your own actions today: where have you tried to produce something perfect (good deeds, relationships, character) while relying on your own deficient power? How does this truth redirect your trust?
?
Life is a condition for knowledge and power. Your very existence is a gift from the Living One. How would your day change if you began it consciously acknowledging that every move you make depends on the life He gives you?

Refutation of Opposing Views on Divine Attributes
Nasafi refutes opposing views on life and pre-eternal knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes those who deny that life is a condition for knowledge and those who deny God's pre-eternal knowledge.

Core Idea
Nasafi refutes both the Salihi/Karramiyya and Jahm/Zarariyya using the same proof.

After establishing that God must have life, power, and knowledge, Nasafi directly addresses two opposing views. The first claims life is not a condition for knowledge; the second claims God was not knowing in pre-eternity. He shows both are invalid because they contradict the necessity derived from contingent things.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi refutes two groups.
2
Identify the first group and their claim
Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi and the Karramiyya denied that life is a condition for knowledge. Understand why Nasafi's proof refutes them.
3
Identify the second group and their claim
Jahm and al-Zarariyya denied that God was knowing in pre-eternity, claiming He created knowledge for Himself later. See how the proof applies.
4
Understand the unified refutation
Both groups are refuted by the same rational argument: contingent things require an originator with power, knowledge, and life, and these attributes must be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يتصف بها، لاتصف بضدها، وضدها نقائص ، والناقص لا يوجد الكامل. ويقول: إن المحدثات توجب اتصاف محدثها بالقدرة والعلم إذ لا فعل يأتي بدون القدرة، ولا إحكام يكون بدون العلم، وكما لا يتصور وجود شيء بدون القدرة، والعلم، لا يتصور وجوده إلا من حي بل إن الحياة شرط في ثبوت العلم والقدرة. إذ الموت والجمادية مضادان للعلم والقدرة. وبهذا الكلام يكون الرد على أبي الحسين الصالحي والكرامية اللذين يقولان: إن الحياة ليست بشرط في ثبوت .العلم. ويذكر رأي جهم - أن الله لم يكن في الأزل عالما، حتى خلق لنفسه ،علما، وكذا رأي الزرارية الذي يشبه رأي جهم، ويناقش آراءهما ليبطلها، ويثبت أزلية علم الله وقدرته، وحياته.
He does not possess [the attributes] without being described by their opposite, and their opposite are deficiencies, and the deficient does not produce the perfect. He says: contingent things necessitate that their originator be described with power and knowledge, for no action occurs without power, and no perfect work occurs without knowledge. And just as it is inconceivable for something to exist without power and knowledge, it is inconceivable for it to exist except from a living being; indeed life is a condition for the affirmation of knowledge and power. For death and inanimateness are opposites to knowledge and power. This speech constitutes a refutation of Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi and the Karramiyya who say: life is not a condition for the affirmation of knowledge. And he mentions the opinion of Jahm — that God was not knowing in pre-eternity until He created for Himself knowledge, and likewise the opinion of al-Zarariyya which resembles the opinion of Jahm, and he discusses their opinions to invalidate them, and affirms the eternity of God's knowledge, power, and life.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage begins with a proof based on the principle of necessity: if a being lacks attributes, it would be described by their opposites, which are deficiencies, and a deficient being cannot produce perfect things. Therefore, contingent things (muhdathat) require that their originator possess power and knowledge, because no action occurs without power and no perfect work without knowledge. Moreover, power and knowledge require life as a condition, since death and inanimateness are opposite to them. Hence the Creator must be living. Nasafi then uses this proof to refute Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi and the Karramiyya who claimed that life is not a condition for knowledge. He also refutes Jahm and al-Zarariyya who denied that God was knowing in pre-eternity, arguing that He created knowledge for Himself later. Through this, Nasafi establishes the eternity of God's knowledge, power, and life.

Correction: Nasafi uses rational argument from contingent things to refute both groups.

God's Knowledge of Non-Existent Things
Nasafi argues for God's knowledge of non-existent and refutes Hisham ibn al-Hakam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi argues that God knows non-existent things and refutes the opposing view through a dialectical argument.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of non-existent things is necessary for prophecy and divine information.

Nasafi engages with a key theological debate: can God know things that do not yet exist? The opponents claim that knowledge can only attach to existent things. Nasafi counters with a powerful dialectical argument rooted in the Quranic statements about future events. This passage demonstrates the rational necessity of divine omniscience encompassing non-existent entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a dialectical style with questions and answers.
2
Identify the initial positions
Find Nasafi's own claim: God knows His essence, attributes, and beyond, including non-existent things. Then find the opposing view of Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Hisham ibn Amr who deny knowledge of non-existent.
The key term 'المعدوم' (the non-existent) appears here.
3
Understand the first refutation
Nasafi argues that denying knowledge of non-existent leads to the absurd conclusion that perfect actions occur without the agent's knowledge. Grasp why this is impossible.
4
Follow the dialectical exchange
Map out the series of questions: Did God inform about future events? If no, disbelief. If yes, did He inform while knowing? If not knowing, His report is unreliable. If knowing, was the thing existent or non-existent? If existent, sophistry; if non-existent, they admit the non-existent is known.
This is the core argument.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that the opponents are forced to admit that God knows non-existent things, which validates Nasafi's position.
Consider how this argument relies on the Quranic texts about future events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عن علمه بما وراء ذاته في الأزل فيقول : إنه كان عالما بذاته تعالى وصفاته ومما وراء ذلك، وجوز دخول المعدوم تحت علم الله. وقال هشام بن الحكم وهشام بن عمرو : لم يكن الله عالما بما وراء ذاتي لأن تعلق العلم بالمعدوم محال ويرد النسفي على هذه الآراء بإبطالها قائلا: من لم يجوز العلم بالمعدوم جعل وجود الأفعال المحكمة لا عن علم لفاعلها بها، وهو محال ويسأل قائلا: هل أخبر الله عما يكون في المستقبل؟ فإن قالوا: لا . كفروا لإنكارهم نصوص القرآن. وإن قالوا نعم . سئلوا هل أخبر وهو عالم أم وهو غير عالم؟ فإن قالوا بالثانية لا يعرف صحة خبره، وبالتالي يؤدي هذا إلى إنكار ما ورد في القرآن وإن قالوا بالأولى، قيل لهم : أكان ما أخبر عنه موجودا أم كان معدوما؟ فإن قالوا موجودا فقد ادعوا وجود البعث بعد الموت مباشرة وهذه سفسطة. وإن قالوا: كان معدوما ، أبطلوا مذهبهم، واعترفوا بكون المعدوم معلوما.
As for His knowledge of what is beyond His Essence in pre-eternity: he says that He was knowing of His Essence, exalted, and His attributes, and what is beyond that, and he permitted the inclusion of the non-existent under God's knowledge. Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Hisham ibn Amr said: God was not knowing of what is beyond His Essence, because the attachment of knowledge to the non-existent is impossible. Nasafi refutes these views by invalidating them, saying: Whoever does not permit knowledge of the non-existent makes the existence of perfect actions be without their agent's knowledge, which is impossible. And he asks, saying: Did God inform about what will be in the future? If they say no, they disbelieve due to their denial of the texts of the Quran. If they say yes, they are asked: Did He inform while knowing or while not knowing? If they say the latter, the truthfulness of His report is not known, and this leads to denial of what is in the Quran. If they say the former, it is said to them: Was what He informed about existent or non-existent? If they say existent, they claim the existence of resurrection immediately after death, and this is sophistry. If they say it was non-existent, they invalidate their own doctrine and admit that the non-existent is known.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage deals with God's knowledge of things other than Himself in pre-eternity, specifically the knowledge of non-existent things (ma'dum). Nasafi first states his position: God was knowing of His essence, attributes, and everything else, and He knows non-existent things. Then he presents the opposing view of Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Hisham ibn Amr, who denied that God knows non-existent things, claiming that knowledge cannot attach to non-existence. Nasafi refutes them by noting that denying knowledge of the non-existent would imply that perfect actions occur without the agent's knowledge, which is absurd. He then employs a dialectical argument: Did God inform about future events? If the opponent denies this, they reject the Quran. If they affirm, they are asked whether God informed while knowing or not. If not knowing, His report is unreliable. If knowing, then was the thing informed about existent or non-existent? If they claim it was existent, that leads to the false claim that resurrection occurs immediately after death. If they admit it was non-existent, they have contradicted their own position and acknowledged that the non-existent is known.

Key Terms
المعدوم
علم الله
هشام بن الحكم
هشام بن عمرو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge can only attach to existent things, so God cannot know the non-existent.
Correction: If God cannot know the non-existent, then His reports about future events (like resurrection) would be unreliable, contradicting the Quran.

Checkpoint
What is the key question Nasafi asks to refute those who deny God's knowledge of the non-existent?
A) Did God create the world?
B) Did God inform about future events?
C) Is God all-powerful?
D) Do prophets exist?

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge encompasses non-existent things, as proven by the Quranic reports about future events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge includes all future events—including your choices tomorrow—does that make you feel trapped or liberated? Why?
?
Nasafi argues that denying God's knowledge of the non-existent leads to disbelief in the Quran. Are there any parts of the Quran you have doubted because they speak of things you cannot see or verify? How does this lesson challenge that doubt?
?
When you pray for something that hasn't happened yet, do you truly believe that God already knows it and that it is already written? If yes, how should that affect the sincerity and persistence of your du'a?

Nasafi's Position: Attributes Neither Identical Nor Distinct
Nasafi states his position on attributes and critiques opponents' understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's distinctive position that divine attributes are neither identical to the essence nor distinct from it, and why he rejects both extremes.

Core Idea
Attributes are neither the essence nor other than it — a middle path beyond identity and distinction.

Nasafi begins by critiquing the inadequate Quranic understanding of his opponents. Then he poses the key question: Are attributes identical to Allah's essence or separate? His answer avoids both extremes, establishing a nuanced position that becomes a hallmark of Maturidi theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice how Nasafi first criticizes opponents' Quranic understanding, then states his position.
2
Identify the two rejected extremes
The two extremes are: attributes identical to essence (ayn al-dhat) and attributes distinct from essence (ghayr al-dhat). Nasafi rejects both.
3
Understand the middle position
Nasafi's position is that attributes are neither identical nor distinct. This means they are real but not separate entities—a nuanced view.
This is a key Maturidi doctrine.
4
Ponder why this matters
This position preserves tawhid while affirming the reality of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد إقامة الدلالة العقلية يفند كلام الخصوم، ويبين أن فهمهم للقرآن قاصر . ويتحدث النسفي بعد ذلك عن الصفات هل هي عين الذات أو غير الذات؟ ويجيب أنها لا عين الذات، ولا غير الذات، ويأتى بشبه المعتزلة، ويعرضها بأمانته المنهجية من ذلك أن الصفة إذا لم تكن عين الذات كانت غير الذات لا محالة، والقول بالأشياء المتغايرة في الأزل ينافي التوحيد، وأنها لو كانت ثابتة لكانت أزلية، ولو كانت أزلية كانت قديمة، والقول بهذا يؤدي إلى القول بآلهة كثيرة، ومن ذلك أيضا - أن الصفات لو ثبتت لكانت باقية ضرورة، ولا وجه إلى القول ببقاء كل صفة ببقاء يقوم بها، لاستحالة قيام الصفة بالصفة.
After establishing the rational proof, he refutes the speech of the opponents and shows that their understanding of the Quran is deficient. Then Nasafi discusses the attributes: Are they identical to the essence or distinct from the essence? He answers that they are neither identical to the essence nor distinct from it. And he brings the plausibilities (shubah) of the Mu'tazila, presenting them with his methodological honesty. Among them: that if an attribute is not identical to the essence, it is necessarily distinct from it, and the assertion of distinct entities in pre-eternity contradicts tawhid. And that if they were established, they would be eternal, and if eternal, they would be pre-eternal (qadim), and asserting this leads to asserting many gods. And also among them: that if the attributes were established, they would necessarily be permanent (baqiya), and there is no way to say that each attribute remains by a permanence (baqa') subsisting in it, because it is impossible for an attribute to subsist in an attribute.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage introduces the key theological issue of the relationship between divine attributes and essence. First, Nasafi criticizes his opponents' understanding of the Quran as inadequate. Then he states his own position: attributes (sifat) are neither identical to the essence (ayn al-dhat) nor distinct from it (ghayr al-dhat). This is a nuanced middle position. He then presents, with methodological honesty, the objections of the Mu'tazila. The first objection: if attributes are not identical to the essence, they must be distinct, and distinct entities in pre-eternity contradict tawhid (monotheism). Second: if attributes are eternal, they would be pre-eternal (qadim), leading to belief in many gods. Third: if attributes were established, they would be permanent (baqiya), and one cannot say that each attribute subsists by a permanence (baqa') of its own, because an attribute cannot subsist in another attribute. These are the main shubah (plausible objections) that Nasafi will later refute.

Key Terms
عين الذات
غير الذات
الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Nasafi's position is ambiguous or that he is simply avoiding the question.
Correction: Nasafi clearly rejects both identity and distinctness, affirming a real distinction without separation. This is a precise theological stance.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the relationship between divine attributes and the essence?
A) They are identical.
B) They are distinct.
C) Neither identical nor distinct.
D) They are created.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's core teaching is that divine attributes are real but not separate from the essence — they are neither 'ayn nor ghayr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's mercy or power, do you naturally treat them as separate 'things' in your mind, or do you reduce them to empty labels? Which extreme does your heart lean towards, and how does Nasafi's middle path challenge that tendency?
?
Nasafi accuses his opponents of having a deficient understanding of the Quran. If you were to honestly examine your own understanding of verses about Allah's attributes, where might you be falling into the same deficiency? What practical step will you take today to deepen that understanding?
?
The statement 'attributes are neither identical to the essence nor distinct from it' leaves a mystery. Are you comfortable with that mystery, or does it make you uneasy? How does your response reveal what you truly believe about Allah's transcendence?

Mu'tazila Objections to Nasafi's Position
Nasafi presents three Mu'tazila objections against his position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main objections raised by the Mu'tazila against the view that attributes are neither identical nor distinct.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila argue that non-identical implies distinct, that eternal attributes imply many gods, and that attribute-permanence leads to absurdity.

After stating his position, Nasafi honestly presents the strongest objections from the Mu'tazila. These shubah (plausible arguments) challenge the coherence of the middle position. Understanding them is crucial to appreciating the later refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Read the Arabic passage listing the three shubah. Notice how Nasafi presents them as plausible arguments.
2
Identify the three distinct arguments
The first argues that non-identity implies distinctness, which harms tawhid. The second argues that established attributes are eternal, leading to many gods. The third argues that attribute permanence is incoherent.
These are classic Mu'tazila objections.
3
Understand each objection's logic
For each, understand the logical chain: what premise leads to what conclusion.
4
Reflect on the overall thrust
All objections aim to show that affirming distinct attributes endangers tawhid or leads to logical absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد إقامة الدلالة العقلية يفند كلام الخصوم، ويبين أن فهمهم للقرآن قاصر . ويتحدث النسفي بعد ذلك عن الصفات هل هي عين الذات أو غير الذات؟ ويجيب أنها لا عين الذات، ولا غير الذات، ويأتى بشبه المعتزلة، ويعرضها بأمانته المنهجية من ذلك أن الصفة إذا لم تكن عين الذات كانت غير الذات لا محالة، والقول بالأشياء المتغايرة في الأزل ينافي التوحيد، وأنها لو كانت ثابتة لكانت أزلية، ولو كانت أزلية كانت قديمة، والقول بهذا يؤدي إلى القول بآلهة كثيرة، ومن ذلك أيضا - أن الصفات لو ثبتت لكانت باقية ضرورة، ولا وجه إلى القول ببقاء كل صفة ببقاء يقوم بها، لاستحالة قيام الصفة بالصفة.

And also among them: that if the attributes were established, they would necessarily be permanent (baqiya), and there is no way to say that each attribute remains by a permanence (baqa') subsisting in it, because it is impossible for an attribute to subsist in an attribute.

Commentary

Then he states his own position: attributes (sifat) are neither identical to the essence (ayn al-dhat) nor distinct from it (ghayr al-dhat). This is a nuanced middle position. Correction: Nasafi presents these objections with methodological honesty to later refute them. He does not endorse them.

A) Attributes are created.
B) Eternal attributes imply many gods.
C) Attributes are identical to essence.
D) Attributes are distinct.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila raise three key objections against the 'la ayn wa la ghayr' position, all centering on the threat to tawhid and logical consistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Mu'tazila objections, do you feel your certainty in tawhid waver? Are you willing to sit with the tension until you understand the refutation, or do you tend to dismiss such arguments too quickly?
?
Imagine you encounter a friend who raises one of these objections against the Islamic understanding of Allah's attributes. Will you be able to engage honestly without knowing Nasafi's reply? What will you do to equip yourself?
?
Do you ever rely solely on logical reasoning about Allah, forgetting that His attributes transcend human analogy? If the Mu'tazila's logic seems compelling to you, are you willing to humble your intellect before the received tradition of the salaf?

Nasafi's Trilemma Against the Mu'tazila
Nasafi presents a trilemma to the Mu'tazila, forcing a choice between three inconsistent positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical trilemma Nasafi presents to the Mu'tazila regarding the analogy between the witnessed and unseen worlds, and why each option undermines their position.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila are trapped: full analogy refutes them, no analogy denies God, partial analogy contradicts itself.

Nasafi, a Maturidi theologian, engages with the Mu'tazila on the issue of divine attributes. He presents a trilemma that exposes the inconsistency of their reasoning about the relationship between the created world and the Creator. In this passage, he forces them to choose between three positions, each of which leads to a dead end for their own doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the trilemma
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the three options Nasafi presents.
2
Identify each option
Note how Nasafi presents three mutually exclusive choices: full analogy, no analogy, or partial analogy.
3
Analyze the trap
For each option, see why it fails from the Mu'tazila perspective: first abandons their doctrine, second denies God's existence, third is contradictory.
4
Reflect on the argument
Consider why this trilemma is an effective refutation and what it reveals about the Mu'tazila's inconsistent methodology.
The key is that they cannot consistently apply or reject analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنسفي يفند هذه الآراء بالعقل والسمع، وهو في رده يستعرض شبها كثيرة ظهرت لأبي على الجبائي، وللعلاف، ولأبي هاشم، وللكعبي، ويقول: نقول للمعتزلة. إنكم بين أمور ثلاثة : إما أن تسووا بين الشاهد والغائب في كل ذلك، وتقولوا: إن قدرة الصانع ثابتة بدلالة المفعول، وعلمه ثابت بدلالة أحكام المفعول، وفي هذا ترك مذهبكم، وإما أن تفرقوا بين الشاهد والغائب في كل ذلك فتقولوا: إن المفعول وإن دل على علمه ففي الغائب لا يدل على شيء. وهذا قمة الإنكار لوجود الصانع، وإما أن تسووا بين الشاهد والغائب في حق دلالته على القدرة والعلم، وتقولوا وإن كان ذلك دليلا عليهما في الشاهد فليس بدليل عليهما في الغائب. فتتناقضوا. أحدهما مع وفي رده عليهم يقول كعادته - التي أحسب أنه لم يلتزم بها منهجيا حينما اشترط على نفسه الإيجاز : لا حاجة بنا إلى الاشتغال بتحديد الغيرين، لأن الخصوم هم الذين يريدون نفي الصفات بعلة أنها لو كانت ثابتة لكانت أغيارا للذات وإثبات غير الله تعالى في الأزل محال، ويقول : إنه لن يشتغل بمنع ذلك إلا على طريقة التبرع، فيحدد الغيرين أنهما الموجودان اللذان يصح وجود عدم الآخر، وأن شيئا مما ذكره الخصوم لا يصلح أن يكون حدا، لأنهما كانا غيرين، لأنهما عرضان لثبوت المغايرة بين الباري، وبين العالم، ولا لأنهما جسمان، لثبوت التغاير بين الأعراض والأجسام، وبين الأجسام والأجسام، وما كانا غيرين، لقيام مغايرة بينهما لجريان التغاير بين الأعراض، وإن كان يستحيل قيام (م - تبصرة الأدلة جـ ١ )
And Nasafi refutes these views with reason and revelation, and in his refutation he reviews many plausibilities that appeared to Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i, al-'Allaf, Abu Hashim, and al-Ka'bi. He says: We say to the Mu'tazila: You are among three matters: either you equate the witnessed and the unseen in all that, and say: the power of the Maker is established by the indication of the effect, and His knowledge is established by the indication of the perfectedness of the effect, and in this is abandoning your doctrine; or you differentiate between the witnessed and the unseen in all that, and say: the effect, even if it indicates His knowledge, in the unseen it indicates nothing, and this is the peak of denial of the existence of the Maker; or you equate between the witnessed and the unseen with respect to its indication of power and knowledge, and say: even if that is a proof for them in the witnessed, it is not a proof for them in the unseen, and thus you contradict yourselves. One of them with. And in his refutation of them, he says, as is his custom — which I think he did not methodically adhere to when he imposed conciseness on himself: we have no need to occupy ourselves with defining the two others (al-ghayrayn), because the opponents are the ones who want to negate the attributes on the pretext that if they were established they would be others (aghyar) to the essence, and affirming other than God in pre-eternity is impossible. He says: he will not occupy himself with preventing that except in a voluntary manner. He defines the two others as the two existents such that the non-existence of the other is valid. And nothing that the opponents mentioned is valid as a definition, because they are not two others because they are two accidents on account of the affirmation of difference between the Creator and the world, nor because they are two bodies on account of the affirmation of difference between accidents and bodies, and between bodies and bodies. And they are not two others because there is difference between them due to the occurrence of difference between accidents, even if it is impossible for the subsistence of (margin note - Tabsirat al-Adilla vol. 1).
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage contains Nasafi's refutation of the Mu'tazila arguments. First, he states that he refutes them with both reason (aql) and revelation (sam'). Then he presents a trilemma to the Mu'tazila: they face three options. First, if they make a complete analogy between the witnessed world and the unseen (God) in all respects, then they must accept that the effect indicates the Maker's power and knowledge in both realms, which would force them to affirm the attributes — this contradicts their own doctrine (which denies attributes). Second, if they completely deny any analogy between the witnessed and unseen, then they have no evidence for God's existence at all, which is the height of denial. Third, if they make a partial analogy but then claim that the effect indicates attributes only in the witnessed but not in the unseen, they contradict themselves. Thus the Mu'tazila are trapped. Then Nasafi says there is no need to define 'ghayr' (other), but he does so voluntarily. He defines 'ghayrayn' as two existents such that the non-existence of the other is possible. This definition does not apply to God and His attributes because the attributes cannot be nonexistent. He then criticizes the opponents' definitions, showing they are invalid.

Key Terms
الشاهد والغائب
المفعول
دلالة
الأمور الثلاثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Nasafi rejects all analogy (qiyas) between witnessed and unseen.
Correction: Nasafi only rejects the Mu'tazila's inconsistent application; he uses analogy himself when it supports the truth.

Checkpoint
What is the third option in Nasafi's trilemma for the Mu'tazila?
A) Complete analogy leading to affirming attributes.
B) Complete denial of any analogy, leading to atheism.
C) Partial analogy, claiming the effect indicates power and knowledge only in the witnessed world, not the unseen.
D) Accepting that the effect indicates nothing at all.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's trilemma forces the Mu'tazila to either abandon their doctrine, deny God's existence, or fall into contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself applying a standard to others that you do not apply to yourself, just as the Mu'tazila accepted analogy for the witnessed but rejected it for the unseen? Where in your own life do you selectively use reasoning to avoid a hard truth? What would it cost you to be consistent?
?
When you consider the evidence for God's existence and attributes, do you ever catch yourself making excuses to avoid the full implications? Are you willing to follow the proof wherever it leads, even if it challenges comfortable assumptions? What would it mean for you to be consistent like the first option demands?
?
The third option is contradictory—do you have any contradictions in your own faith that you have not resolved? Is there a gap between what you profess and how you live? Will you ask Allah to show you where you are inconsistent and give you the courage to fix it?

Defining 'Ghayr': Nasafi's Refutation of a Mu'tazila Objection
Nasafi defines 'ghayrayn' and refutes the Mu'tazila's objection about attributes being 'others'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's definition of 'two others' (al-ghayrayn) and why the Mu'tazila's objection that divine attributes would be 'others' is invalid.

Core Idea
Two things are 'others' only if one can exist without the other—God's attributes fail this test.

The Mu'tazila argued that God's attributes, if real, would be 'other' than God's essence, implying multiple eternal entities. Nasafi responds by providing a precise definition of 'other' (ghayr) and showing that the Mu'tazila's own definitions are flawed. He then argues that the attributes do not meet the criteria for being 'others'.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic where Nasafi defines 'ghayrayn' as two existents such that non-existence of the other is possible.
2
Understand the key point
Two things are 'others' only if one can exist without the other. Since God's attributes cannot be nonexistent, they are not 'others'.
3
Apply to attributes
See why this definition excludes God's attributes: they are inseparable from His essence.
4
Critique opponents' definitions
Note how Nasafi dismisses the Mu'tazila's alternative definitions based on accidents or bodies, showing they lead to absurdities.
