Eternality of Divine Irada and Refutation of Karramiyya
Nasafi discusses the eternality of irada, refutes Karramiyya, and affirms one irada for all.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ahl al-Haqq affirm an eternal irada for God, the Karramiyya position of a temporal irada for creation, and why Nasafi accuses them of heresy, along with the unity of God's will for all objects.

Core Idea
God's will is one, eternal, and encompasses all objects of will.

The third question addresses whether God's irada is eternal or temporally originated. The people of truth say eternal, but the Karramiyya claim that God in eternity has a capacity to will (muridiyya) and creates the world by a temporal irada. Nasafi refutes this as impossible and even charges them with heresy. He then affirms that God wills all things with one single irada.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the positions: Ahl al-Haqq, Karramiyya, and Nasafi's refutation and conclusion.
2
Compare the two positions on eternality
Ahl al-Haqq say eternal irada. Karramiyya say eternal capacity (muridiyya) but temporal irada for creation. Understand the difference.
Muridiyya is the abstract noun for 'willingness' or ability to will.
3
Understand Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi shows the impossibility of a temporal accident (irada) subsisting in God's essence, as God's essence is not a substrate for temporal things. He harshly accuses them of heresy.
4
Grasp the final point about one irada
Nasafi concludes that God's will is one for all objects of will, refuting the Karramiyya's claim of multiple iradas for each object.
This reinforces divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسأل النسفي : هل الله تعالى مريد بإرادة أم بغير إرادة؟ ويجيب بأن من قال بالإرادة قال : إنه مريد بها أما الحسين النجار فقد قال: إنه مريد بذاته، معتمدا في كلامه على المعتزلة النافين للصفات، ولا شك في فساد قوله. ويسأل هل إرادته تعالى قائمة بذاته أم لا بذاته؟ فأهل الحق يقولون : إنه تعالى مريد بإرادة قائمة بذاته. أما العلاف والجبائيان فقالوا: إن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة حادثة لا في محل. ويناقشهم النسفي مبينا استحالة قيام العرض بنفسه. ثم يسأل: هل إرادته تعالى أزلية أم حادثة؟ ويجيب بأن أهل الحق قالوا: إنه مريد بإرادة أزلية قائمة به. أما الكرامية فيقولون : إنه في الأزل مريد بمريديَّة، ويقصدون بذلك قدرته على الإرادة، وأنه تعالى قد أحدث العالم بإرادة حادثة. ويناقشهم مبينا استحالة قيام الحادث بذاته تعالى وهنا يقسو عليهم فيتهمهم بالإلحاد. وبعد هذا يذكر النسفي أن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة واحدة كل المرادات خلافا للكرامية القائلين إن لكل مراد إرادة تحققت في ذاته.
And al-Nasafi asks: Is Allah the Exalted willing by an irada or without an irada? And he answers: Those who affirm al-irada said that He is willing by it; but al-Husayn al-Najjar said: He is willing by His essence, relying in his speech on the Mu'tazila who negate attributes, and there is no doubt about the corruption of his statement. And he asks: Is His irada the Exalted subsisting in His essence or not in His essence? The people of truth say: He the Exalted is willing by an irada subsisting in His essence. As for al-Allaf and al-Jubba'iyan (the two Jubba'is), they said: Allah the Exalted is willing by a temporally originated irada that is not in a locus (la fi mahall). And al-Nasafi discusses them, showing the impossibility of an accident subsisting by itself. Then he asks: Is His irada the Exalted eternal or temporally originated? And he answers that the people of truth said: He is willing by an eternal irada subsisting in Him. As for the Karramiyya, they say: In eternity He is willing by a muridiyya (will-ness), meaning by that His ability to will, and that He the Exalted brought the world into existence by a temporally originated irada. And he discusses them, showing the impossibility of the temporal originating in His essence the Exalted, and here he treats them harshly, accusing them of heresy. Commentary
This passage addresses three connected questions about the modality of divine irada. First, is God willing by an irada or by His essence? Nasafi answers that the majority say by an irada, but al-Husayn al-Najjar said by essence, relying on Mu'tazila who negate attributes; Nasafi declares this corrupt. Nasafi refutes them by arguing that an accident cannot subsist by itself (it requires a substrate). Third, is the irada eternal or temporal? Ahl al-Haqq say eternal, subsisting in God. The Karramiyya say God in eternity is willing by a 'muridiyya' (capacity to will) and that He created the world by a temporal irada; Nasafi shows the impossibility of a temporal accident subsisting in God's essence, and even charges them with heresy. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that multiple objects of will require multiple wills in God.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that God's will is one and eternal, encompassing all objects of will.

Checkpoint
What do the Karramiyya believe about God's will in relation to the creation of the world?
A) God creates by an eternal irada
B) God creates by a temporally originated irada
C) God creates by His essence without irada
D) God creates by many eternal iradas

Key Takeaway
God's will is one, eternal, and subsisting in His essence; the Karramiyya position of a temporal will is impossible and heretical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking that God's will changes depending on your circumstances or your prayers? How does the fact that God's will is one, eternal, and unchanging challenge that thought? What can you do today to anchor your faith in His unchanging will rather than in your fluctuating feelings?
?
The Karramiyya attempted to rationalize God's will in a way that preserved His transcendence but ended up compromising His perfection. Are there any areas in your understanding of God where you are tempted to 'make it easier' or more human-like? Commit to studying the attributes of Allah with sincerity, even if it challenges your comfort zone.
?
Nasafi did not hold back from accusing the Karramiyya of heresy for their view on irada. How seriously do you take correct belief about Allah's attributes? Is there any deviation in your own belief, however subtle, that you need to correct? What step will you take this week to ensure your aqeedah is sound?

The Definition of Divine Wisdom (Hikma) and the Wise (Hakim)
Nasafi defines hikma linguistically and technically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand both the linguistic and technical definitions of hikma as used by Nasafi and the mutakallimun.

Core Idea
Wisdom is not just knowledge but purposeful action aligned with intention.

Before we can affirm that Allah is wise, we need a clear definition of wisdom. Nasafi begins with the linguistic meaning—knowledge and mastery—and then presents the technical definition used by the theologians, particularly al-Ash'ari, which ties wisdom to the intention of the agent. This lesson clarifies these foundational definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Notice the progression from linguistic to technical meanings. Read the Arabic once without analysis, then the translation.
Pay attention to the shift from language to technical usage.
2
Identify the linguistic definitions
List the three linguistic definitions of hikma mentioned: knowledge, firm control, restraining oneself from desires.
The core linguistic meaning is knowledge of things in their realities and proper placement.
3
Understand the mutakallimun's definition
Focus on the definition attributed to al-Ash'ari: hikma in an action is its occurrence according to the intention of the agent.
This contrasts with safah (folly), which is action contrary to intention.
4
Distinguish hikma from safah
Compare the two: hikma is action in line with the agent's purpose, safah is action that goes against it.
This distinction is crucial for understanding divine wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في أن صانع العالم حكيم. يبين النسفي معنى الحكمة والحكيم في اللغة فيقول : الحكمة العلم، والحكيم العالم ، وقيل : الحكيم هو المحكم للشيء، وقيل: الحكيم هو الذي يمنع نفسه عن هواها إلخ. والحكمة معرفة الأشياء بحقائقها، ووضعها مواضعها. أما علي رأي المتكلمين فإن الحكمة فى الفعل وقوعه على قصد فاعله، والسفه وقوعه على خلاف قصد فاعله وينسب هذا القول للأشعري.
The discussion that the Maker of the world is wise. Al-Nasafi explains the meaning of al-hikma and al-hakim in language, saying: al-hikma is knowledge, and al-hakim is the knower; and it is said: al-hakim is the one who firmly controls a thing; and it is said: al-hakim is the one who restrains himself from his desires, etc. And al-hikma is knowledge of things in their realities, and placing them in their proper places. As for according to the opinion of the mutakallimin, al-hikma in an action is its occurrence according to the intention of the agent, and al-safah (folly) is its occurrence contrary to the intention of the agent, and this statement is attributed to al-Ash'ari.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic that the Creator of the world is wise (hakim). Nasafi first gives linguistic definitions: hikma means knowledge, and hakim means the knower; other definitions include the one who firmly controls a thing, or the one who restrains his own desires. The core meaning is knowledge of things in their true realities and placing them in their proper contexts. Then he presents the mutakallimin's technical definition, specifically attributed to al-Ash'ari: hikma in an action means it occurs according to the agent's intention (qasd), while safah (folly) means it occurs contrary to the agent's intention. This frames divine wisdom as purposeful creation.

الحكيم
السفه
المتكلمين
الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think hikma is only knowledge without considering purpose.
Correction: Nasafi shows that hikma also involves placing things in their proper contexts and, in the technical sense, acting according to intention.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimin, what is the definition of hikma in an action?
A) The action being eternal
B) The action occurring according to the agent's intention
C) The action being good for all
D) The action being possible

Key Takeaway
Divine wisdom (hikma) means that Allah's actions occur according to His intention and are placed in their proper contexts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If divine wisdom means everything happens according to Allah's intention, what event in your life have you been dismissing as meaningless or random? How can you start seeing it as part of His wise plan starting today?
?
The passage says hikma is 'placing things in their proper places.' Where have you placed something—your time, your love, your trust—in a wrong place? What would it actually cost you to put it back where it belongs?
?
If you truly believed that every trial is an action of God with a purpose, would your reaction to your current difficulty be different? What is stopping you from living that belief right now?

Divine Will: Views of Ahl al-Haqq and Mu'tazila
Nasafi states the Ahl al-Haqq and Mu'tazilite views and the question of eternality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Ahl al-Haqq and Mu'tazilite positions on the nature and temporality of divine will.

Core Idea
Divine will is either eternal and subsistent or originated and without a locus.

This lesson introduces the foundational disagreement about God's will. You will first read the Ahl al-Haqq's assertion that God is willing with an eternal will subsisting in His essence, and then the Mu'tazilite view that the will is originated and not in a locus. Pay attention to the key terms and the logical question that frames the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the two opposing positions.
Note that the Mu'tazilites are represented by al-‘Allāf and the two Jubba’īs.
2
Identify the two positions
Locate the statements of Ahl al-Haqq and the Mu'tazilites. Observe how each describes the will (eternal vs. originated, subsisting in essence vs. not in a locus).
3
Understand the question
The passage raises a question about eternality or origination; see how the answer reinforces Ahl al-Haqq's position.
This question is central to the debate.
4
Reconstruct the argument
Summarize the two views in your own words. Then consider why each school chose its position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أهل الحق يقولون : إنه تعالى مريد بإرادة قائمة بذاته. أما العلاف والجبائيان فقالوا: إن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة حادثة لا في محل. ثم يسأل: هل إرادته تعالى أزلية أم حادثة؟ ويجيب بأن أهل الحق قالوا: إنه مريد بإرادة أزلية قائمة به. أما الكرامية فيقولون : إنه في الأزل مريد بمريديَّة، ويقصدون بذلك قدرته على الإرادة، وأنه تعالى قد أحدث العالم بإرادة حادثة. ثم يذكر النسفي أن الله تعالى مريد بإرادة واحدة كل المرادات خلافا للكرامية القائلين إن لكل مراد إرادة تحققت في ذاته.
The people of truth say: He (Most High) is willing (murīd) with a will (irāda) subsisting in His essence. As for al-‘Allāf and the two Jubba’īs, they said: God (Most High) is willing with an originated will (irāda ḥāditha) not in a locus. Then he asks: Is His will (Most High) eternal or originated? And he answers that the people of truth said: He is willing with an eternal will (irāda azaliyya) subsisting in Him. As for the Karramiyya, they say: He is in pre-eternity willing with a willing-ness (murīdiyya), meaning thereby His power over will, and that He (Most High) originated the world with an originated will. Then al-Nasafi mentions that God (Most High) is willing with one will for all objects of will, contrary to the Karramiyya who say that for each object of will there is a will realized in His essence.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition on the nature of divine will (irāda). It begins by stating the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, i.e., the Ash‘ari and Maturidi schools): God is willing with an eternal will that subsists in His essence. Then it contrasts the Mu‘tazilite view of al-‘Allāf and the two Jubba’īs, who held that God's will is originated (ḥāditha) and not in a locus (lā fī maḥall). The passage then raises the question of whether the will is eternal or originated, answering that Ahl al-Haqq affirm an eternal will subsisting in God. The Karramiyya are presented as holding an intermediate view: God is willing in pre-eternity with a 'willing-ness' (murīdiyya) which is a power to will, but the actual will that originated the world is originated. Finally, al-Nasafi states his own conclusion: God is willing with one will for all objects of will, refuting the Karramiyya who posit multiple wills in God's essence.

Key Terms
إرادة
مريد
أهل الحق
العلاف
الجبائيان
حادثة
أزلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Mu'tazila also held that God's will is eternal.
Correction: This passage clearly shows they considered it originated (ḥāditha), not eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the Ahl al-Haqq as presented in this passage, how is God's will described?
A) Originated and not in a locus
B) Eternal and subsisting in His essence
C) A power to will in pre-eternity
D) Multiple wills for each object of will

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Haqq affirm an eternal will subsisting in God's essence, while the Mu'tazilites posit an originated will without a locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's will is eternal and unchanging, or do you sometimes act as if His will can be swayed by your desires? How would your prayers change if you deeply internalized that His will has always been and will never change?
?
The Mu'tazila reduced God's will to an originated accident lacking a substrate. Have you ever, even subtly, rationalized away a divine attribute because it did not fit your human logic? Examine your heart: are there aspects of God's perfection you struggle to accept?
?
If God's will is eternal and subsisting in His essence, then every event in your life has been eternally decreed by Him. Are you at peace with His decree, or do you still resist it? What would it take for you to fully surrender to a will that has always been?

Divine Will: The Karramiyya Position and the Refutation
Nasafi presents the Karramiyya view and refutes it with one eternal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya view on divine will and how Nasafi refutes it with the doctrine of one eternal will.

Core Idea
God wills all objects with a single eternal will, not multiple originated wills.

This lesson completes the discussion on divine will. After the Mu'tazilites, we encounter the Karramiyya who propose a middle position: God has a pre-eternal 'willingness' but uses originated wills for each act. The passage ends with Nasafi's decisive refutation: God's will is one and eternal for all objects of will.

Guided Path
1
Read the Karramiyya view
Understand their distinction between pre-eternal willingness (murīdiyya) and originated will for creation.
Murīdiyya is a capacity to will, not the attribute itself.
2
Identify the refutation
Find Nasafi's statement that God is willing with one will for all objects of will, contrary to the Karramiyya who posit multiple wills.
3
Compare with earlier positions
See how the refutation aligns with Ahl al-Haqq's eternal will and rejects both Mu'tazilite origination and Karramiyya multiplicity.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why positing multiple wills would conflict with divine simplicity and eternity.

Defining Wisdom: Linguistic and Theological Perspectives
Nasafi defines wisdom (hikma) and discusses theological positions on God's wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple definitions of wisdom (hikma) in classical Islamic theology, including linguistic, Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, and Maturidi views, and why the attribution of wisdom to God is understood differently by different schools.

Core Idea
Wisdom is not just knowledge but also placing things in their proper places and having praiseworthy consequences.

The term 'wisdom' (ḥikma) is central to discussions about God's attributes, but its meaning is not uniform. Al-Nasafi begins by clarifying the linguistic roots of the word, then moves to theological definitions offered by different schools. This passage sets the stage for understanding how God can be described as 'wise' and what that implies about His actions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic definitions
Read the opening sentences where Nasafi gives several meanings of ḥikma and ḥakīm in the Arabic language. Notice the progression from simple to more complete definitions.
The term 'muḥkim' (one who perfects) connects to the idea of mastery.
2
Learn the theological definitions
Identify the three theological definitions: the Ash'ari view (action according to intention), the Mu'tazila view (action with benefit), and Nasafi's own view (action with praiseworthy consequence).
3
Contrast Nasafi's view with others
Compare Nasafi's definition with the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila definitions. Ask yourself what makes his definition distinct.
Nasafi emphasizes the outcome (praiseworthy consequence) rather than benefit or intention.
4
Grasp the pre-eternity of God's wisdom
Read the final sentence where Nasafi states that God is described with wisdom in pre-eternity, and note the Ash'ari distinction between wisdom as knowledge (eternal) and wisdom as action (temporal).
This is a key point of difference between Maturidi and Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في أن صانع العالم حكيم. يبين النسفي معنى الحكمة والحكيم في اللغة فيقول : الحكمة العلم، والحكيم العالم ، وقيل : الحكيم هو المحكم للشيء، وقيل: الحكيم هو الذي يمنع نفسه عن هواها إلخ. والحكمة معرفة الأشياء بحقائقها، ووضعها مواضعها. أما علي رأي المتكلمين فإن الحكمة فى الفعل وقوعه على قصد فاعله، والسفه وقوعه على خلاف قصد فاعله وينسب هذا القول للأشعري. أما المعتزلة فيقولون: الحكمة كل فعل فيه نفع إما للفاعل أو لغير الفاعل. أما رأيه فيقول : إن الحكمة كل ما له عاقبة حميدة والسفه ما خلا عن العاقبة الحميدة. والله تعالى موصوف بالحكمة في الأزل خلافا للأشعري الذي يقول : إن أريد بها العلم فهو كذلك ، وإن أريد بها الفعل فلا.
The discourse on that the Maker of the world is wise. Al-Nasafi explains the meaning of wisdom (ḥikma) and the wise (ḥakīm) in language, saying: al-ḥikma is knowledge (al-‘ilm), and al-ḥakīm is the knower (al-‘ālim). And it is said: al-ḥakīm is the one who perfects (al-muḥkim) a thing. And it is said: al-ḥakīm is the one who restrains his soul from its desires, etc. And al-ḥikma is the knowledge of things in their realities and placing them in their proper places. As for according to the view of the theologians (al-mutakallimīn), al-ḥikma in action is its occurrence according to the intention of the agent, and al-safah (foolishness) is its occurrence contrary to the intention of the agent, and this statement is attributed to al-Ash‘arī. As for the Mu‘tazila, they say: al-ḥikma is every action in which there is benefit either for the agent or for other than the agent. As for his (al-Nasafi's) view, he says: al-ḥikma is everything that has a praiseworthy consequence, and al-safah is everything devoid of praiseworthy consequence. And God (Most High) is described with wisdom in pre-eternity, contrary to al-Ash‘arī who says: if it is intended as knowledge, then it is so; but if it is intended as action, then not.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a theological definition and discussion of wisdom (ḥikma) as an attribute of God. It begins by presenting linguistic definitions: wisdom is knowledge, the wise is the knower, or the one who perfects a thing, or the one who restrains his soul from desires. Then it gives a more precise definition: wisdom is knowing things in their realities and placing them in their proper places. The passage then shifts to the technical usage of the theologians (mutakallimīn). For them, wisdom in action means that the action occurs according to the agent's intention; its opposite (safah, foolishness) is when the action occurs contrary to intention. This view is attributed to al-Ash‘arī. Next, the Mu‘tazilite view is presented: wisdom is any action that brings benefit to the agent or someone else. Finally, al-Nasafi gives his own view: wisdom is anything with a praiseworthy consequence, and foolishness is the lack of such consequence. The passage concludes with a key theological point: God is described with wisdom in pre-eternity, contrary to al-Ash‘arī who distinguishes: if 'wisdom' means knowledge, then it is eternal; but if it means action, then it is not eternal (since actions are temporal).

Key Terms
حكمة
حكيم
محكم
السفه
المتكلمون
الأشعري
المعتزلة
العاقبة الحميدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wisdom necessarily involves benefit to someone.
Correction: Nasafi defines wisdom as having a praiseworthy consequence, which is broader than benefit. An action can be wise even if it does not benefit anyone, as long as its outcome is praiseworthy.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's own view stated in this passage, what is wisdom (al-ḥikma)?
A) Knowledge of things and placing them in proper places
B) Action that benefits the agent or another
C) Everything that has a praiseworthy consequence
D) Action that occurs according to the agent's intention

Key Takeaway
Wisdom, according to al-Nasafi, is defined by its praiseworthy consequence, and God is eternally wise in this sense, contrary to the Ash'ari view that limits eternal wisdom to knowledge alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make decisions, do you evaluate them by whether they lead to a praiseworthy consequence in the sight of Allah, or do you focus only on immediate benefit or personal gain? What one area of your life needs to realign with true wisdom as defined here?
?
Al-Nasafi teaches that God is described with wisdom in pre-eternity—His wisdom is not dependent on time or outcomes. How does this truth shape your trust in Allah's decree, especially when you face trials whose good consequences are not yet visible?
?
The passage distinguishes wisdom from foolishness (safah) based on consequence. In what ways do your daily habits reflect either wisdom or foolishness? Commit to one specific change today to pursue actions with praiseworthy consequences.

The Beginning of the Discourse on Seeing God
Nasafi opens the topic of seeing God, states Ahl al-Haqq position and opponent groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Haqq position on seeing God, the sects that oppose it, and one key scriptural argument used by the opponents.

Core Idea
Seeing God is possible in reason and necessary by revelation.

This lesson marks the start of a major theological discussion: can God be seen? Nasafi immediately lays down the orthodox position and identifies the opposing groups. He then begins to engage with their proofs, starting with a crucial Qur'anic verse that the opponents use to deny the possibility of vision. This passage sets the stage for a nuanced debate about the nature of divine vision.

Guided Path
1
State the Ahl al-Haqq position
Read the first sentence and identify what Ahl al-Haqq affirm regarding seeing God – its rational permissibility and its necessity by revelation, and when and where the believers will see Him.
2
Identify the opposing sects
Find the names of the three groups that deny the possibility of seeing God. Notice that they think it leads to something impossible for Allah.
These are Mu'tazila, Najjariyya, and Zaydiyya.
3
Analyze the opponents' revealed argument
Read the Qur'anic verse they cite and the reasoning that follows. Understand why they think this verse negates vision entirely.
The verse is 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' – vision comprehends Him not.
4
Observe the beginning of the rational argument
The passage starts to mention the opponents' rational argument about vision in the witnessed world having causes and conditions. Notice that the passage cuts off; the full argument will be completed later.
This is just the introduction of the rational objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في جواز رؤية الله تعالى في العقل ووجوبها بالسمع. ويسأل في البداية: هل يرى الله تعالى؟ ويجيب بأن أهل الحق جوزوا ذلك ويراه المؤمنون في الآخرة، بعد دخولهم الجنة، خلافا للمعتزلة والنجارية والزيدية القائلين بعدم جواز ذلك، لأنهم يحسبون أن ذلك يؤدي إلى ما يستحيل على الله تعالى. ويذكر أدلة الخصوم ومنها قوله تعالى: ﴿ لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٠٣] فقد تمدح سبحانه بنفي الإدراك لذاته، وإثبات عكس ذلك يؤدي إلى النقص. ومن أدلتهم العقلية التي ذكرها أن للرؤية في الشاهد علة وشروطا، ولا
The discourse on the permissibility of seeing God (Most High) in reason and its necessity by revelation. And he asks at the beginning: Is God (Most High) seen? And he answers that the people of truth permitted it, and that the believers will see Him in the afterlife, after entering Paradise, contrary to the Mu‘tazila, the Najjariyya, and the Zaydiyya, who say that it is not permissible, because they think that leads to what is impossible for God (Most High). And he mentions the arguments of the opponents, among them His saying (Most High): {Vision comprehends Him not} (al-An‘am 103). For He (Glorified) praised Himself by negating comprehension (idrāk) from His essence, and affirming the opposite leads to deficiency. And among their rational arguments which he mentioned is that vision (ru’ya) in the witnessed world has a cause and conditions, and not...
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage introduces the theological discussion on the beatific vision (ru’yat Allāh). It starts by stating the topic: the rational permissibility of seeing God and its necessity by revelation. Then it raises the question 'Is God seen?' and answers with the position of Ahl al-Haqq: God can be seen, and the believers will see Him in the afterlife after entering Paradise. This opposes the Mu‘tazila, Najjariyya, and Zaydiyya, who deny its possibility. The text explains that the opponents argue that seeing God would imply something impossible for God. Then it begins to list their evidence: first a Qur’anic verse, {Vision comprehends Him not}, arguing that God praises Himself by negating comprehension (idrāk), and affirming the opposite would imply deficiency. Then it mentions rational arguments: in the witnessed world (shāhid), vision has causes and conditions. The passage cuts off mid-sentence, but the argument is that these conditions cannot apply to God, so vision is impossible. The passage sets up the refutation that will follow.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' means that God cannot be seen at all.
Correction: Ahl al-Haqq interpret 'idrak' as comprehensive perception, not mere vision. God can be seen but not fully comprehended.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when will the believers see God according to Ahl al-Haqq?
A) In this world
B) In the afterlife after entering Paradise
C) Only in their dreams
D) Never, as it is impossible

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Haqq affirm that seeing God is possible and will occur for believers in Paradise, contrary to several rationalist sects who rely on the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' as a key proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed you will see Allah with your own eyes in Paradise, how would your worship and longing for Him change starting today? Where would you begin?
?
When have you let your limited reason reject a revealed truth because it seemed impossible to you? Are you willing to submit your intellect to revelation, even when it humbles you?
?
The opponents feared that affirming vision would imply deficiency in Allah. Is there any attribute or action of Allah that you shy away from affirming because it makes you uncomfortable? Will you trust Allah's description of Himself over your own assumptions?

Definitions of Wisdom: Mu'tazila, Author, and al-Ash'ari
Al-Nasafi records different definitions of wisdom and critiques the views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different definitions of wisdom (al-ḥikma) held by the Muʿtazila, al-Nasafī, and al-Ashʿarī, and the key theological point about God's wisdom being pre-eternal.

Core Idea
Wisdom's meaning determines whether God is described by it eternally.

This passage from al-Nasafī's Tabsirat al-Adilla records three distinct definitions of wisdom: one from the Muʿtazila, one from the author himself, and a note on al-Ashʿarī's position. The discussion touches on the deeper issue of God's attributes and whether wisdom is inherent to His essence. Let's examine each view and see what is at stake.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: three views presented sequentially.
2
Identify the three definitions
List the definitions given by the Muʿtazila, the author (al-Nasafī), and al-Ashʿarī. Note how each defines wisdom differently.
The Muʿtazila focus on benefit; the author on outcome; al-Ashʿarī distinguishes knowledge from action.
3
Understand the Ashʿarī distinction
Al-Ashʿarī is not denying God's wisdom outright. He only denies it if wisdom means a temporal action. If it means knowledge, he affirms it eternally.
This nuance is crucial to avoid misunderstanding the Ashʿarī position.
4
Reflect on pre-eternal attribute
Consider why al-Nasafī insists that God is described with wisdom in pre-eternity (fī l-azal). This ties to the Māturīdī view that all divine attributes are eternal.
Contrast with Ashʿarī who limits eternal wisdom to knowledge only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المعتزلة فيقولون: الحكمة كل فعل فيه نفع إما للفاعل أو لغير الفاعل. أما رأيه فيقول: إن الحكمة كل ما له عاقبة حميدة والسفه ما خلا عن العاقبة الحميدة. ويحيل الحديث في هذا الموضوع إلى مسألة التعديل والتجوير وإلى كتاب (٧٤) له قال إنه يكتبه في هذا الموضوع وقد بحثت عن هذا الكتاب فلم أهتد إليه. والله تعالى موصوف بالحكمة في الأزل خلافا للأشعري الذي يقول: إن أريد بها العلم فهو كذلك، وإن أريد بها الفعل فلا.
As for the Mu'tazila, they say: Wisdom (al-ḥikma) is every action in which there is benefit, either for the agent or for someone other than the agent. As for his view—i.e., the author's—he says: Wisdom is everything that has a praiseworthy consequence, and foolishness (al-safah) is whatever is devoid of a praiseworthy consequence. He refers the discussion on this topic to the issue of declaring God just and unjust (al-taʿdīl wa-l-tajwīr) and to a book (74) which he said he was writing on this topic—and I searched for this book but did not find it. And God Most High is described as possessing wisdom (al-ḥikma) pre-eternally, contrary to al-Ashʿarī, who says: If what is meant by it is knowledge, then it is so; but if what is meant by it is action, then not.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents three definitions of wisdom (al-ḥikma) and notes a key theological difference. It begins by recording the Muʿtazilī definition: wisdom is any action that brings benefit either to the agent or to another. The author then gives his own view: wisdom is whatever has a good outcome, and foolishness is the opposite. He mentions that the detailed discussion of this belongs to the topics of declaring God just or unjust (al-taʿdīl wa-l-tajwīr) and refers to a book he wrote, though the editor notes it could not be found. Finally, the author states that God is described as having wisdom pre-eternally, disagreeing with al-Ashʿarī, who distinguishes: if wisdom means knowledge, then God has it eternally; but if it means action, then He does not (since actions are temporal). This highlights a key Ashʿarī-Māturīdī divergence on the attribute of wisdom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ashʿarī denies that God is wise at all.
Correction: Al-Ashʿarī affirms wisdom if it means knowledge, but denies it if it means action (since actions are temporal).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is al-Ashʿarī's position on God's wisdom?
A) He affirms it unconditionally.
B) He denies it entirely.
C) He affirms it if it means knowledge, but denies it if it means action.
D) He says it is created in time.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ashʿarī's distinction between wisdom as knowledge (eternal) and wisdom as action (temporal) shows that the debate over divine attributes often hinges on definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila define wisdom as every action that brings benefit. When you face trials in your life that seem to bring no benefit, do you still trust that God is wise? How does your definition of wisdom affect your patience with His decree?
?
The author defines wisdom as having a praiseworthy consequence, not necessarily immediate benefit. Is there an area in your life where you demand immediate benefit from God, rather than trusting that His actions have a praiseworthy outcome you cannot yet see? What would it take for you to surrender that demand?
?
Al-Ashʿarī distinguishes between wisdom as knowledge (eternal) and wisdom as action (temporal). Do you sometimes confuse God's eternal attributes with His temporal acts—judging His character by a single event rather than by His unchanging nature? How can you align your view of Him with the truth that His wisdom is eternal, even when His actions seem puzzling?

The Proof from Musa's Request (Quran 7:143)
Author presents the proof from Musa's request and Allah's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the story of Musa (AS) and his request to see Allah provides a Quranic argument for the permissibility (jawaz) of seeing Allah.

Core Idea
Allah did not declare ru'ya impossible; He only deferred it.

The story of Musa (AS) asking to see Allah is a pivotal proof for the Ahl al-Sunna. This lesson examines the Quranic verses, the author's reasoning, and the key lesson that Allah's response did not declare vision impossible, but merely that it would not happen in this world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author extracts multiple arguments from a single verse.
The passage uses concise reasoning—pay attention to each point.
2
Identify the three arguments
The author presents three distinct indicators: (1) Allah did not say it is impossible, (2) vision is attached to a contingent event, (3) lack of rebuke. Find each in the text.
3
Understand the tajalli interpretation
Read the explanation of 'tajalli' from al-Nasafi. Consider how this supports the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife.
This interpretation shows that created beings can be given the capacity to see Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من ذلك قول موسى عليه السلام: رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٤٣] ففي الآية اعتقاد الله لأن الجهل منتف عن الرسول وإخبار الله إياه أنه لن يراه، ولم يخبر الله سبحانه أن رؤيته مستحيلة مع أن الحاجة للبيان واضحة، فدل على جواز رؤيته تعالى، كذلك فإن الله تعالى علق الرؤية على أمر جائز وهو استقرار الجبل، وتعليق الفعل على ما هو جائز الوجود يدل على جوازه، وكذلك فإنه تعالى ما أيأس موسى ولا عاتبه حينما سأله رؤيته، وهذا يدل على جواز الرؤية، ولو كان الأمر مستحيلا أو جهلا من موسى عليه السلام، لكان العتاب، وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ فَلَمَّا تَجَلَّى [الأعراف: ١٤٣] والتجلي هو الظهور، ويفسره النسفي نقلا عن ابن فورك عن أبي الحسن الأشعري: أن الله تعالى أعطى الجبل روحا وقدرة على الرؤية فرأى الله. ويذكر خلال ذلك تفسيرات المعتزلة الآية رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنْظُرْ إِلَيْكَ [الأعراف: ١٤٣] من ذلك قولهم : إن موسى عليه السلام لم يطلب الرؤية، وإنما طلب آية يستدل بها على الله ويفسد النسفي هذا الكلام بأكثر من دليل. ومن ذلك : أن موسى لم يطلب الرؤية لنفسه، وإنما طلبها قومه، لأنهم يطلبونها فأراد بالطلب أن يبين الله لهم استحالتها. ويبطل النسفي هذا الكلام. ومن كلام النسفي أيضا على جواز رؤيته تعالى قوله تعالى: ﴿ وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إلى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ [القيامة : ۲۲ ، ۲۳]، ويعرض النسفي الكلام حول هذه الآية بالتفصيل، مستشهدا بكلام النحويين والشعراء، مثبتا أن المراد بها النظر لا الانتظار . وأيضا من الأدلة قوله تعالى: لِلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَى وَزِيَادَةٌ } [يونس: ٢٦] وقد روى في خبر أن الزيادة هي النظر إلى الله تعالى، ويدعم كلامه بأن واحدا وعشرين من الصحابة الأجلاء رووا حديث الرؤية عن رسول الله ويذكرهم بأسمائهم، وقد بحثت عنهم جميعا فوجدت أنهم ثقات كما هو مبين في التحقيق.
From that is the saying of Musa, peace be upon him: 'My Lord, show me so that I may look at You' [Al-A'raf: 143]. In the verse is the belief of Allah because ignorance is negated from the prophet, and Allah's telling him that he will not see Him, but Allah, exalted, did not inform him that seeing Him is impossible, despite the need for clarification being clear. So this indicates the permissibility of seeing Him, exalted. Likewise, Allah, exalted, attached vision to a permissible matter, which is the settling of the mountain, and attaching an action to that whose existence is permissible indicates its permissibility. And likewise, He did not cause Musa to despair nor did He rebuke him when he asked for His vision, and this indicates the permissibility of vision. If the matter had been impossible or ignorance on the part of Musa, peace be upon him, there would have been rebuke. And likewise His saying, exalted: 'So when He manifested Himself [to the mountain]' [Al-A'raf: 143], and 'tajalli' is appearance. And al-Nasafi explains it, reporting from Ibn Furak from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari: that Allah, exalted, gave the mountain a spirit and the ability to see, so it saw Allah. And he mentions during that the interpretations of the Mu'tazila of the verse 'My Lord, show me so that I may look at You' [Al-A'raf: 143]; among that is their saying: that Musa, peace be upon him, did not request vision, but rather requested a sign by which to infer Allah. And al-Nasafi invalidates this speech with more than one proof. And from that: that Musa did not request vision for himself, but his people requested it, because they were asking for it, so he intended by the request that Allah show them its impossibility. And al-Nasafi nullifies this speech. And from al-Nasafi's speech also on the permissibility of seeing Him, exalted: His saying, exalted: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' [Al-Qiyama: 22-23]. And al-Nasafi presents speech about this verse in detail, citing the speech of grammarians and poets, affirming that what is meant by it is 'looking' not 'waiting'. And also from the proofs is His saying, exalted: 'For those who do good is the best and an increase' [Yunus: 26]. And it has been narrated in a report that the increase is looking at Allah, exalted. And he supports his speech by the fact that twenty-one of the noble companions narrated the hadith of vision from the Messenger of Allah, and he mentions them by name, and I have investigated all of them and found that they are reliable, as is explained in the verification.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents Quranic proofs for the permissibility (jawaz) of seeing Allah (ru'ya Allah). The author begins with the story of Musa (Moses) from Surah Al-A'raf (7:143). He argues that since Musa asked to see Allah, and Allah did not tell him it was impossible (only that he would not see Him in this world), and since Musa was a prophet free from ignorance, this indicates that seeing Allah is possible in principle. Furthermore, Allah attached the possibility of seeing Him to a contingent event (the mountain settling), which shows that vision is not intrinsically impossible. The author also notes that Allah did not rebuke Musa for asking, which would have been necessary if the request were ignorant or impossible. The interpretation of 'tajalli' (manifestation) is then explained via Ibn Furak and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari: Allah gave the mountain spirit and sight so that it saw Him, demonstrating that seeing Allah is possible for beings with the appropriate capacity. The author then refutes Mu'tazila interpretations that deny that Musa requested actual vision, using multiple proofs. Next, the author cites Surah Al-Qiyama (75:22-23) where faces are described as 'looking at their Lord', and affirms through linguistic analysis that this means visual sight, not waiting. Finally, he cites Surah Yunus (10:26) where 'increase' is interpreted as looking at Allah, supported by hadith reports from numerous companions. The passage is rich in scriptural evidence and refutations of opposing views.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الجواز
التجلي
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing Allah is impossible because He is not a body.
Correction: The Quran does not declare ru'ya impossible; it only deferred it for Musa, indicating possibility in the hereafter. The mountain incident shows that Allah can grant the capacity to see Him.

Checkpoint
What does the story of Musa indicate about seeing Allah?
A) It is impossible.
B) It is possible but not in this world.
C) Musa actually saw Allah.
D) Only prophets can see Allah.

Key Takeaway
The story of Musa shows that Allah did not declare vision impossible, thus it is permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask Allah for something that seems impossible, do you expect Him to rebuke you or to respond with wisdom? What would you do if He only deferred your request, as He did for Musa? Will you persist in hope or give up in despair?
?
The mountain, a lifeless object, was given the ability to see Allah. Do you believe Allah can grant you capacities beyond your current nature to know Him? Or do you limit His power by your own expectations? Are you ready to be transformed in ways you cannot imagine?
?
If Allah had declared ru'ya impossible, that would be the end of it. But He didn't. What does it say about your own relationship with Allah that you may be settling for less than what He has made possible for you? Are you content with veiling yourself from seeking His vision in this life and the next?

Refutation of Mu'tazila Interpretations of Musa's Request
Author presents and refutes two Mu'tazila interpretations of Musa's request.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila attempts to reinterpret Musa's request and how al-Nasafi refutes them.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila reinterpretations collapse under textual and rational scrutiny.

The Mu'tazila, denying the possibility of seeing Allah, offered alternative interpretations of Musa's request. This lesson presents two such interpretations and al-Nasafi's refutations, reinforcing the Sunni position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claims
Read the passage that presents the two Mu'tazila interpretations. Note how they try to reconcile the verse with their denial of ru'ya.
The first interpretation claims Musa asked for a sign, not vision. The second claims his people asked, not him.
2
Understand the refutations
Al-Nasafi invalidates both claims with multiple proofs. Think about why these refutations are effective.
The refutations rely on the plain meaning of the text and the prophetic integrity.
3
Reflect on the methodology
Consider how the author uses logic and textual evidence to defend the Sunni position against rationalist reinterpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من ذلك قول موسى عليه السلام: رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٤٣] ففي الآية اعتقاد الله لأن الجهل منتف عن الرسول وإخبار الله إياه أنه لن يراه، ولم يخبر الله سبحانه أن رؤيته مستحيلة مع أن الحاجة للبيان واضحة، فدل على جواز رؤيته تعالى، كذلك فإن الله تعالى علق الرؤية على أمر جائز وهو استقرار الجبل، وتعليق الفعل على ما هو جائز الوجود يدل على جوازه، وكذلك فإنه تعالى ما أيأس موسى ولا عاتبه حينما سأله رؤيته، وهذا يدل على جواز الرؤية، ولو كان الأمر مستحيلا أو جهلا من موسى عليه السلام، لكان العتاب، وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ فَلَمَّا تَجَلَّى [الأعراف: ١٤٣] والتجلي هو الظهور، ويفسره النسفي نقلا عن ابن فورك عن أبي الحسن الأشعري: أن الله تعالى أعطى الجبل روحا وقدرة على الرؤية فرأى الله. ويذكر خلال ذلك تفسيرات المعتزلة الآية رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنْظُرْ إِلَيْكَ [الأعراف: ١٤٣] من ذلك قولهم : إن موسى عليه السلام لم يطلب الرؤية، وإنما طلب آية يستدل بها على الله ويفسد النسفي هذا الكلام بأكثر من دليل. ومن ذلك : أن موسى لم يطلب الرؤية لنفسه، وإنما طلبها قومه، لأنهم يطلبونها فأراد بالطلب أن يبين الله لهم استحالتها. ويبطل النسفي هذا الكلام. ومن كلام النسفي أيضا على جواز رؤيته تعالى قوله تعالى: ﴿ وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إلى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ [القيامة : ۲۲ ، ۲۳]، ويعرض النسفي الكلام حول هذه الآية بالتفصيل، مستشهدا بكلام النحويين والشعراء، مثبتا أن المراد بها النظر لا الانتظار . وأيضا من الأدلة قوله تعالى: لِلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَى وَزِيَادَةٌ } [يونس: ٢٦] وقد روى في خبر أن الزيادة هي النظر إلى الله تعالى، ويدعم كلامه بأن واحدا وعشرين من الصحابة الأجلاء رووا حديث الرؤية عن رسول الله ويذكرهم بأسمائهم، وقد بحثت عنهم جميعا فوجدت أنهم ثقات كما هو مبين في التحقيق.
From that is the saying of Musa, peace be upon him: 'My Lord, show me so that I may look at You' [Al-A'raf: 143]. In the verse is the belief of Allah because ignorance is negated from the prophet, and Allah's telling him that he will not see Him, but Allah, exalted, did not inform him that seeing Him is impossible, despite the need for clarification being clear. So this indicates the permissibility of seeing Him, exalted. Likewise, Allah, exalted, attached vision to a permissible matter, which is the settling of the mountain, and attaching an action to that whose existence is permissible indicates its permissibility. And likewise, He did not cause Musa to despair nor did He rebuke him when he asked for His vision, and this indicates the permissibility of vision. If the matter had been impossible or ignorance on the part of Musa, peace be upon him, there would have been rebuke. And likewise His saying, exalted: 'So when He manifested Himself [to the mountain]' [Al-A'raf: 143], and 'tajalli' is appearance. And al-Nasafi explains it, reporting from Ibn Furak from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari: that Allah, exalted, gave the mountain a spirit and the ability to see, so it saw Allah. And he mentions during that the interpretations of the Mu'tazila of the verse 'My Lord, show me so that I may look at You' [Al-A'raf: 143]; among that is their saying: that Musa, peace be upon him, did not request vision, but rather requested a sign by which to infer Allah. And al-Nasafi invalidates this speech with more than one proof. And from that: that Musa did not request vision for himself, but his people requested it, because they were asking for it, so he intended by the request that Allah show them its impossibility. And al-Nasafi nullifies this speech. And from al-Nasafi's speech also on the permissibility of seeing Him, exalted: His saying, exalted: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' [Al-Qiyama: 22-23]. And al-Nasafi presents speech about this verse in detail, citing the speech of grammarians and poets, affirming that what is meant by it is 'looking' not 'waiting'. And also from the proofs is His saying, exalted: 'For those who do good is the best and an increase' [Yunus: 26]. And it has been narrated in a report that the increase is looking at Allah, exalted. And he supports his speech by the fact that twenty-one of the noble companions narrated the hadith of vision from the Messenger of Allah, and he mentions them by name, and I have investigated all of them and found that they are reliable, as is explained in the verification.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents Quranic proofs for the permissibility (jawaz) of seeing Allah (ru'ya Allah). The author begins with the story of Musa (Moses) from Surah Al-A'raf (7:143). He argues that since Musa asked to see Allah, and Allah did not tell him it was impossible (only that he would not see Him in this world), and since Musa was a prophet free from ignorance, this indicates that seeing Allah is possible in principle. Furthermore, Allah attached the possibility of seeing Him to a contingent event (the mountain settling), which shows that vision is not intrinsically impossible. The author also notes that Allah did not rebuke Musa for asking, which would have been necessary if the request were ignorant or impossible. The interpretation of 'tajalli' (manifestation) is then explained via Ibn Furak and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari: Allah gave the mountain spirit and sight so that it saw Him, demonstrating that seeing Allah is possible for beings with the appropriate capacity. The author then refutes Mu'tazila interpretations that deny that Musa requested actual vision, using multiple proofs. Next, the author cites Surah Al-Qiyama (75:22-23) where faces are described as 'looking at their Lord', and affirms through linguistic analysis that this means visual sight, not waiting. Finally, he cites Surah Yunus (10:26) where 'increase' is interpreted as looking at Allah, supported by hadith reports from numerous companions. The passage is rich in scriptural evidence and refutations of opposing views.

Key Terms
الرؤية
المعتزلة
التأويل
الرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Musa did not actually request to see Allah; he requested a sign.
Correction: The plain text shows Musa asked for vision (anẓur ilayka), and the prophet would not ask for something impossible or irrelevant.

Checkpoint
What is the first Mu'tazila interpretation of Musa's request?
A) Musa requested vision for his people.
B) Musa requested a sign, not actual vision.
C) Musa requested vision but was denied.
D) Musa did not request anything.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila reinterpretations by showing they contradict the plain meaning and prophetic integrity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse, 'My Lord, show me so that I may look at You,' does your heart lean towards accepting its plain meaning, or do you find yourself wanting to reinterpret it to avoid a difficult implication? What does that reveal about your trust in Allah's words?
?
The Mu'tazila twisted Musa's request to protect their preconceived notion that Allah cannot be seen. Is there any belief about Allah that you hold so tightly that you would twist clear scripture to preserve it? Will you submit to the text, or will you make the text submit to you?
?
Imagine you are in Musa's place: would you dare to ask Allah for the impossible with a sincere heart, trusting that He can do anything? Or would you, like the Mu'tazila claim, only ask as a lesson for others? When was the last time you made a bold, vulnerable request in your du'a, expecting a real answer from your Lord?

The Proof from Surah Al-Qiyama: Faces Looking at Their Lord
Author presents linguistic proof from Surah Al-Qiyama for ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة' proves the permissibility of seeing Allah through linguistic analysis.

Core Idea
The word 'nazira' in this verse means visual sight, not waiting.

Surah Al-Qiyama describes the believers' faces as 'looking at their Lord.' Al-Nasafi uses grammatical and poetic evidence to show that the word 'nazira' means visual sight, not 'waiting' as some claim. This lesson examines that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its translation
Read the Arabic verse and the literal translation. Note the key word 'nazira' and its preposition 'ila'.
2
Understand the linguistic argument
Al-Nasafi cites grammarians and poets to prove that 'nazira ila' means looking with the eyes. Consider the difference from 'muntazira' (waiting).
The context of 'nādira' (radiant) also supports visual sight.
3
Appreciate the conclusiveness
This linguistic evidence leaves no room for reinterpretation. The verse clearly affirms ru'ya in the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من ذلك قول موسى عليه السلام: رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٤٣] ففي الآية اعتقاد الله لأن الجهل منتف عن الرسول وإخبار الله إياه أنه لن يراه، ولم يخبر الله سبحانه أن رؤيته مستحيلة مع أن الحاجة للبيان واضحة، فدل على جواز رؤيته تعالى، كذلك فإن الله تعالى علق الرؤية على أمر جائز وهو استقرار الجبل، وتعليق الفعل على ما هو جائز الوجود يدل على جوازه، وكذلك فإنه تعالى ما أيأس موسى ولا عاتبه حينما سأله رؤيته، وهذا يدل على جواز الرؤية، ولو كان الأمر مستحيلا أو جهلا من موسى عليه السلام، لكان العتاب، وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿ فَلَمَّا تَجَلَّى [الأعراف: ١٤٣] والتجلي هو الظهور، ويفسره النسفي نقلا عن ابن فورك عن أبي الحسن الأشعري: أن الله تعالى أعطى الجبل روحا وقدرة على الرؤية فرأى الله. ويذكر خلال ذلك تفسيرات المعتزلة الآية رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنْظُرْ إِلَيْكَ [الأعراف: ١٤٣] من ذلك قولهم : إن موسى عليه السلام لم يطلب الرؤية، وإنما طلب آية يستدل بها على الله ويفسد النسفي هذا الكلام بأكثر من دليل. ومن ذلك : أن موسى لم يطلب الرؤية لنفسه، وإنما طلبها قومه، لأنهم يطلبونها فأراد بالطلب أن يبين الله لهم استحالتها. ويبطل النسفي هذا الكلام. ومن كلام النسفي أيضا على جواز رؤيته تعالى قوله تعالى: ﴿ وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إلى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ [القيامة : ۲۲ ، ۲۳]، ويعرض النسفي الكلام حول هذه الآية بالتفصيل، مستشهدا بكلام النحويين والشعراء، مثبتا أن المراد بها النظر لا الانتظار . وأيضا من الأدلة قوله تعالى: لِلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَى وَزِيَادَةٌ } [يونس: ٢٦] وقد روى في خبر أن الزيادة هي النظر إلى الله تعالى، ويدعم كلامه بأن واحدا وعشرين من الصحابة الأجلاء رووا حديث الرؤية عن رسول الله ويذكرهم بأسمائهم، وقد بحثت عنهم جميعا فوجدت أنهم ثقات كما هو مبين في التحقيق.
From that is the saying of Musa, peace be upon him: 'My Lord, show me so that I may look at You' [Al-A'raf: 143]. In the verse is the belief of Allah because ignorance is negated from the prophet, and Allah's telling him that he will not see Him, but Allah, exalted, did not inform him that seeing Him is impossible, despite the need for clarification being clear. So this indicates the permissibility of seeing Him, exalted. Likewise, Allah, exalted, attached vision to a permissible matter, which is the settling of the mountain, and attaching an action to that whose existence is permissible indicates its permissibility. And likewise, He did not cause Musa to despair nor did He rebuke him when he asked for His vision, and this indicates the permissibility of vision. If the matter had been impossible or ignorance on the part of Musa, peace be upon him, there would have been rebuke. And likewise His saying, exalted: 'So when He manifested Himself [to the mountain]' [Al-A'raf: 143], and 'tajalli' is appearance. And al-Nasafi explains it, reporting from Ibn Furak from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari: that Allah, exalted, gave the mountain a spirit and the ability to see, so it saw Allah. And he mentions during that the interpretations of the Mu'tazila of the verse 'My Lord, show me so that I may look at You' [Al-A'raf: 143]; among that is their saying: that Musa, peace be upon him, did not request vision, but rather requested a sign by which to infer Allah. And al-Nasafi invalidates this speech with more than one proof. And from that: that Musa did not request vision for himself, but his people requested it, because they were asking for it, so he intended by the request that Allah show them its impossibility. And al-Nasafi nullifies this speech. And from al-Nasafi's speech also on the permissibility of seeing Him, exalted: His saying, exalted: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' [Al-Qiyama: 22-23]. And al-Nasafi presents speech about this verse in detail, citing the speech of grammarians and poets, affirming that what is meant by it is 'looking' not 'waiting'. And also from the proofs is His saying, exalted: 'For those who do good is the best and an increase' [Yunus: 26]. And it has been narrated in a report that the increase is looking at Allah, exalted. And he supports his speech by the fact that twenty-one of the noble companions narrated the hadith of vision from the Messenger of Allah, and he mentions them by name, and I have investigated all of them and found that they are reliable, as is explained in the verification.

?

The Proof from Surah Yunus: 'Increase' as Seeing Allah
Author presents the proof from Surah Yunus and the prophetic interpretation via companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'للذين أحسنوا الحسنى وزيادة' supports the possibility of seeing Allah, based on prophetic interpretation.

Core Idea
The 'increase' in the verse is explained by the Prophet as looking at Allah.

The verse promises the best and an increase for those who do good. The Prophet interpreted the 'increase' as the vision of Allah. This lesson presents that proof and the chain of narrators.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the hadith
Read the verse from Surah Yunus and the commentary that the 'increase' is looking at Allah. Note the emphasis on the hadith.
2
Understand the support from companions
The author lists twenty-one companions who narrated the hadith of ru'ya. This shows the widespread transmission and reliability.
3
Appreciate the strength of the proof
Combining Quranic evidence with mutawatir hadith establishes the possibility of seeing Allah as a certain doctrine.

?

?

?

The Rational Argument for Seeing Allah: Corporeality vs. Self-Subsistence
Rational argument for vision of Allah: cause is self-subsistence, not corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for the permissibility of seeing Allah, based on the cause of visibility being self-subsistence rather than corporeality.

Core Idea
The cause of visibility is self-subsistence (qa'im bi al-dhat), not being a body.

In this passage, the author presents the rational argument for the possibility of seeing Allah. He contrasts the Mu'tazila claim that visibility requires a body with the Maturidi claim that self-subsistence is the cause. He then challenges the Mu'tazila to justify their own causal framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two competing positions on the cause of visibility.
Notice that the author first mentions his method of prioritizing rational proof.
2
Identify the two causal claims
Identify the Mu'tazila claim (corporeality as cause) and the Maturidi claim (self-subsistence as cause). Understand how each leads to a conclusion about seeing Allah.
3
Examine the challenge to the Mu'tazila
Read the author's question to the Mu'tazila: why do they treat visibility as a ruling attached to causes rather than as an attribute of existence? Reflect on the force of this challenge.
This challenge undermines their assumption that visibility is causally linked to bodies.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this argument fits into broader kalam debates about divine attributes. Why is the cause of visibility important for proving that Allah can be seen in the afterlife?
This connects to theological positions on the Beatific Vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ذلك يستدل على جواز الرؤية بالعقل فيقول : كان من الأجدى الاستدلال بالعقل أولا ثم تأييد ذلك بالنقل كما هو منهجه - إلا أنه قدم الأدلة السمعية لما فيها من ثبوت الرؤية وجوازها جميعا. ومن أدلته العقلية على جواز الرؤية : أن الخصوم أثبتوا أن العلة المطلقة للرؤية هي الجسمية، فما كان بجسم كان مرئيا، والله ليس بجسم فلا يكون مرئيا، أما عند النسفي فإن أصحابه قالوا بأن العلة المطلقة للرؤية هي كونه قائما بالذات، فما كان قائما بالذات تجوز رؤيته، وما ليس كذلك يستحيل رؤيته، والله تعالى قائم بالذات فكان جائز الرؤية. ويشرح هذا الكلام مناقشا المعتزلة في كلامهم. ثم بعد ذلك يقول : نسأل المعتزلة لم قلتم : إن تعلق الرؤية بهذه الأجناس تعلق الأحكام بالعلل، دون كون هذه الأجناس من أوصاف الوجود؟ ويطالبون بالدليل فما أجابوا به يهدم عليهم قولهم. ثم يقول : وينبغي أن لا ندعي أن العلة المطلقة للرؤية هي القيام بالذات أيضا، وما ذكره الماتريدي وأصحابه كان على طريقة المسامحة والمساهلة، ولا نشتغل بادعاء شيء علة، فإنهم هم المدعون أن العلة المطلقة للرؤية ما زعموا. فلا معنى للاشتغال بمعارضة العلة بالعلة قبل العجز عن منع دليل الخصوم وهدمه.
And after that he adduces proof for the permissibility of vision by reason, saying: It would have been more appropriate to adduce proof by reason first, then support that by transmission, as is his method — except that he presented the transmitted proofs because in them is both the establishment of vision and its permissibility together. And among his rational proofs for the permissibility of vision: that the opponents affirmed that the absolute cause ('illa mutlaqa) for vision is corporeality (jismiyya); so whatever is a body is visible, and Allah is not a body, so He cannot be seen. As for al-Nasafi, his followers said that the absolute cause for vision is that it is self-subsistent (qa'im bi al-dhat); so whatever is self-subsistent, its vision is permissible, and whatever is not like that, its vision is impossible. And Allah, exalted, is self-subsistent, so He is possible of vision. And he explains this speech, discussing the Mu'tazila in their speech. Then after that he says: We ask the Mu'tazila: Why did you say that the attachment of vision to these genera is the attachment of rulings to causes, without these genera being attributes of existence? And they are asked for proof, and whatever they answer refutes their own statement. Then he says: And it is fitting that we do not claim that the absolute cause for vision is self-subsistence either, and what al-Maturidi and his followers mentioned was by way of concession and facilitation. And we should not occupy ourselves with claiming a cause, for they are the ones who claim that the absolute cause for vision is what they allege. So there is no point in occupying oneself with opposing a cause with a cause before being unable to prevent and refute the proof of the opponents.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents rational arguments (adilla 'aqliyya) for the permissibility of seeing Allah, followed by a methodological note. The author first notes that although his usual method is to start with rational proofs, he here presents transmitted proofs first because they establish both the fact and the possibility of vision. The rational proof is framed as a response to the Mu'tazila who claim that the cause of visibility is being a body, so since Allah is not a body, He cannot be seen. The author's school (the Maturidiyya) instead holds that the cause of visibility is being self-subsistent (qa'im bi al-dhat). Since Allah is self-subsistent, it is possible to see Him. The author then directly addresses the Mu'tazila, asking why they insist that the attachment of vision to certain genera (color, spatial location, etc.) is like the attachment of rulings to causes, rather than these being mere attributes of existence. He challenges them to produce proof, arguing that whatever they answer will undermine their own position. Finally, the author offers a methodological observation: one should not hastily claim a cause for vision (even the Maturidi claim is made with leniency). Instead, the proper approach is to first refute the opponent's proof before proposing an alternative cause. Otherwise, engaging in competing claims of causality is pointless.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that visibility requires a body, so Allah cannot be seen because He is not a body.
Correction: The passage argues that the cause of visibility is self-subsistence, not corporeality. Since Allah is self-subsistent, He can be seen.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi position as presented in the passage, what is the absolute cause ('illa mutlaqa) for the possibility of seeing Allah?
A) Being a body (jismiyya).
B) Being self-subsistent (qa'im bi al-dhat).
C) Being in a direction (jiha).
D) Being an attribute of existence.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi argument for vision of Allah is based on the cause of visibility being self-subsistence, not corporeality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Maturidi argument is based on the attribute of self-subsistence, not physicality. If you truly believe you will see Allah in the afterlife, how is that affecting your priorities today? What would change if you lived with the certainty of that vision?
?
The author chose to present transmitted proofs before rational proofs. Are you more convinced by rational arguments or by revelation when it comes to matters of faith? What does that say about your trust in Allah's words versus your own reasoning?
?
The Mu'tazila are challenged to justify why they treat a correlation as a causal law. What are some assumptions you hold about Allah that you have never critically examined? Will you take time today to investigate whether those assumptions are truly based on revelation or on your own limited understanding?

A Methodological Principle: Refute First, Then Propose Causes
Methodological note: avoid claiming causes prematurely; refute first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of first refuting the opponent's proof before proposing an alternative cause in theological debates.

Core Idea
First refute the opponent's proof; only then propose a cause.

The author concludes with a crucial methodological point: do not hastily claim a cause for vision, even the Maturidi claim was made with leniency. The proper approach is to first refute the opponent's proof; engaging in competing claims of causality before refutation is pointless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about methodology
Read the Arabic and translation. Pay attention to the author's advice: do not claim a cause, and do not oppose a cause with a cause.
2
Understand the reason for not claiming causes
The author says that the Maturidi claim was made as a concession (musamaha), not as a definitive statement. Why? Because the more rigorous approach is to refute the opponent's proof first.
This reflects a deeper principle in kalam: one should not assume a causal framework without first establishing it.
3
Ponder the strategic advice
The author says there is no point in opposing a cause with a cause if you haven't refuted the opponent's proof. Think about why this is effective: it forces the opponent to defend their own ground.
4
Apply the principle
Consider how this principle could be applied in other theological debates, e.g., about divine attributes or free will. Why is it better to first refute the opponent's proof than to present an alternative?

Answering the Objection of Pointing to God
Nasafi answers the objection that visible things can be pointed to.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that if God is visible He must be pointable is invalid, and how Nasafi distinguishes between vision and pointing.

Core Idea
Pointing is due to extension, not visibility.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a key objection raised by the Mu'tazila against the possibility of seeing God. They argue that if God were visible, He could be pointed to like any visible object. Nasafi's answer clarifies the nature of pointing and vision, drawing on the distinction between the visible world and the divine. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of objection and answer.
2
Identify the objection
Find the Mu'tazila argument and Nasafi's initial response referencing the Ash'arites.
The objection is that visibility implies pointability.
3
Understand the key distinction
Note that pointing in the witnessed world is due to extension (tahayyuz), not visibility.
This is the core logical move.
4
Apply to the unseen
Since God is not extended, vision does not imply pointability. Nasafi concludes that vision is an attribute of existence, not extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ذلك يقول : إن لهم شبها أخرى ويذكر بعضها منها : أنه إذا كان الباري مرئيا لجاز أن يشار إليه. ويجيب بأن الأشعرية يجوزون الإشارة إلى الله تعالى لا إلى جهة، ونحن نجوز الرؤية لا في جهة وجواز الإشارة إلى شيء في الشاهد لا لجواز رؤيته بل لكونه متحيزا، فالرؤية من أوصاف الوجود لا من أوصاف التحيز.
And after that he says: They have other doubts and he mentions some of them, including: that if the Creator is visible, then it would be permissible to point to Him. And he answers that the Ash'arites allow pointing to God Most High without direction, and we allow vision without direction, and the permissibility of pointing to something in the witnessed is not due to the permissibility of seeing it, but due to its being extended (mutahayyiz). Thus vision is from the attributes of existence, not from the attributes of extension.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This lesson presents an objection and its answer. The Mu'tazila argue that if God could be seen, He could also be pointed to (ishāra). Nasafi responds by first noting that the Ash'arites allow pointing to God without specifying a direction. Then he clarifies that the permissibility of pointing in the witnessed world is not linked to visibility but to being extended (tahayyuz). Since God is not extended, but He is existent, vision can be without direction. Thus the objection fails because it confuses the conditions of the witnessed with the unseen.

Key Terms

الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God can be seen, He can be pointed to like any physical object.
Correction: Nasafi explains that pointing in the physical world is due to the object being extended, not because it is visible. Since God is not extended, vision does not imply pointability.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi say that the objection about pointing to God is invalid?
A) Pointing is only possible in the unseen
B) Pointing in the witnessed is due to extension, not visibility
C) God cannot be pointed to at all
D) Vision requires pointing

Key Takeaway
The objection that vision implies pointability fails because pointing in the physical world is due to extension, not visibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Verse Negates Encompassment, Not Sight
Nasafi interprets the Quranic verse 'Vision perceives Him not' as negating encompassment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi interprets the verse 'Vision perceives Him not' (Quran 6:103) as negating full encompassment, not vision itself, and why this supports the possibility of seeing God.

Core Idea
The verse denies full encompassment, not ordinary sight.

This lesson unpacks a key Quranic verse often cited by those who deny the beatific vision. Nasafi, following Imam al-Maturidi, argues that the word 'idrāk' in 'lā tudrikuhu al-absār' means 'to fully encompass'—like knowing all limits and boundaries. By clarifying this meaning, the verse no longer negates seeing God, only the idea that we can fully encompass Him by sight.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then the English translation. Notice that Nasafi first critiques the Ash'arite interpretation before presenting the Maturidi view.
The key term 'idrāk' appears several times—notice how it is defined.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Nasafi mentions that the Ash'arites interpret the negation as applying to 'the sights' (al-absār), not to the seers. He rejects this. Then he presents the Maturidi view: the negation applies to 'idrāk' meaning encompassment.
This clarifies that the verse does not negate vision itself.
3
Understand al-Maturidi's analogy
Al-Maturidi compares vision to knowledge: just as negating encompassment (ihāṭa) in knowledge does not negate knowledge itself, so negating idrāk in vision does not negate vision. Knowledge of God is established, but full encompassment is negated.
The analogy shows that ordinary vision is possible even if full encompassing is not.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
The Maturidi interpretation preserves the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife while maintaining His transcendence—He cannot be fully encompassed by any created sight.
This is a classic example of how precise word meanings resolve theological disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقول في آية لا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٠٣] إن الأشعرية يقولون: إن النفي ينصب على الأبصار لا على المبصرين، ويبطل هذا الكلام، ثم يقول: المنفي هنا هو الإدراك بمعنى الإحاطة والشمول، والرؤية غير منتفية، مؤكدا كلامه بما قاله الماتريدي: إن الإدراك هو الوقوف على أطراف الشيء، وحدوده، ونهاياته كالإحاطة فكان الإدراك في الرؤية نازلا منزلة الإحاطة من العلم، ثم بنفي الإحاطة لا ينتفي العلم، فكان العلم هو تبين المعلوم على ما هو به، والإحاطة هي الوقوف على حدود المعلوم ونهاياته، فكان العلم بالله ثابتا والإحاطة منتفية.
And he says regarding the verse 'Vision perceives Him not' [Quran 6:103] that the Ash'arites say that the negation applies to the sights, not to the seers, and he invalidates this speech. Then he says: What is negated here is perception (idrāk) in the sense of encompassment (ihāṭa) and inclusion (shumūl), and vision is not negated. He confirms his statement with what al-Maturidi said: Indeed, idrāk is stopping at the edges of a thing, its limits, and its ends like encompassment. So idrāk in vision is in the position of encompassment in knowledge. Then by negating encompassment, knowledge is not negated. So knowledge is the disclosure of the known as it is, and encompassment is stopping at the limits and ends of the known. Thus knowledge of God is established, and encompassment is negated.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This lesson deals with the interpretation of the key Quranic verse used by the Mu'tazila to deny vision of God: 'Vision perceives Him not' (Quran 6:103). Nasafi first mentions that the Ash'arites interpret the negation to apply to the 'sights' (absār) themselves, not to the act of seeing. He invalidates this position. Then he presents the Maturidi interpretation: the word 'yudrikuhu' means 'perceives' in the sense of 'encompasses' (ihāṭa). The verse negates encompassment, not vision itself. He supports this with al-Maturidi's analogy: just as in knowledge, negating encompassment does not negate knowledge itself; similarly, in vision, negating idrāk (encompassment) does not negate vision. So the verse does not deny that God can be seen, only that He can be fully encompassed by sight.

Key Terms
إدراك
إحاطة
رؤية
علم
الماتريدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' explicitly denies any seeing of God.
Correction: Nasafi shows that 'idrāk' means full encompassment, not ordinary sight, so the verse only negates encompassing God by sight, not the act of seeing itself.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi say is the meaning of 'idrāk' in the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار'?
A) Sight in general
B) Encompassment and inclusion
C) Looking with intent
D) Seeing from a distance

Key Takeaway
The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' negates full encompassment by sight, not the possibility of seeing God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly long to see God in the afterlife, or have you allowed intellectual doubts—like the Mu'tazilite denial—to cool your hope? What would it mean for your worship today if you were certain you will see Him without encompassing Him?
?
When you reflect on God's transcendence, do you mistakenly keep Him distant, as if His majesty prevents closeness? How might this verse, which affirms vision while negating full comprehension, reshape your intimacy with Him?
?
If you cannot fully comprehend God's essence even in the next life, how does that humble your pride in this life? In what area of your faith have you acted as if you have 'encompassed' God's will, and will you now surrender to mystery?
