Everything by God's Will: Obedience and Sin
Al-Nasafi states the principle of universal divine will and distinguishes obedience from sin regarding God's pleasure and command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that everything occurs by God's will, but that obedience and sin differ in that sin is not by His command, pleasure, or love.

Core Idea
All events are willed by God, but He only loves and commands what is good.

Al-Nasafi establishes the foundational Sunni principle that nothing happens outside God's will. However, he carefully distinguishes between two categories: acts of obedience and acts of disobedience. This distinction preserves God's perfection: He wills all that occurs, but His pleasure and command only attach to the good.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: first a universal statement, then a detailed list for obedience, then for sin.
The passage uses a repetitive pattern for emphasis.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the list of attributes that accompany obedience and those that accompany sin. Notice which attributes are shared and which are absent for sin.
Obedience: will, desire, pleasure, love, command, decree, power. Sin: will, desire, decree, ordainment. Not: command, pleasure, love.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why does al-Nasafi include 'pleasure' and 'love' only for obedience? Consider how this protects God from being associated with evil while affirming His sovereignty.
This distinction is key to the Sunni position against the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول النسفي : إن كل شيء حدث بإرادة الله وعلى هذا فكل شيء يقع تحت إرادته تعالى. ثم يعرض آراء المعتزلة، ويقول : الأصل عندنا أن كل حادث حدث بإرادة الله تعالى على أي وصف كان، ثم ما كان في ذلك طاعة فهو بمشيئة الله، وإرادته، ورضاه، ومحبته، وأمره، وقضائه وقدرته، وما كان معصية فهو بمشيئة الله، وإرادته، وقضائه وقدره، وليس بأمره، ولا برضاه، ولا بمحبته. وهنا يذكر اختلافا بين الماتريدية، والأشاعرة وهو هل المحبة والرضا بمعنى الإرادة أم لا؟ يقول الماتريدية: لا، ويقول الأشاعرة: نعم. ويقول : إن الإرادة موافقة للعلم، وعند المعتزلة موافقة للأمر. ثم يعرض بعض الأقوال فيقول : قال عبد الله بن سعيد القطان: إن الله تعالى مريد حدوث كل ما علم حدوثه، ولا يكون في سلطانه إلا ما يريد كونه ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن. وقد اختار الأشعري القول عند التفصيل أن لا يقتصر على القول: أن الله سبحانه أراد الكفر، والزنا بل أزيد عليه قرينة، فأقول : أراد من الكافر الكفر كسبا له قبيحا منه، مذموما. ويقول النسفي : إن رأى الأشعري قريب من رأي أبي منصور الماتريدي،
Al-Nasafi says: Indeed, everything that occurs is by the will of God, and on this basis everything falls under His will, exalted. Then he presents the views of the Mu'tazila, and says: The principle with us is that every event occurs by the will of God Almighty, on whatever description it may be. Then what is in that an obedience (ta'a) is by God's will (mashi'a), His desire (irada), His pleasure (rida), His love (mahaba), His command (amr), His decree (qada') and His power (qudra). And what is a sin (ma'siya) is by God's will, His desire, His decree and His ordainment (qadr), and it is not by His command, nor by His pleasure, nor by His love. And here he mentions a disagreement between the Maturidis and the Ash'aris: namely, are love and pleasure synonymous with desire (irada) or not? The Maturidis say: No, and the Ash'aris say: Yes. And he says: Indeed, desire corresponds to knowledge ('ilm), while with the Mu'tazila it corresponds to command. Then he presents some statements and says: 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan said: Indeed, God Almighty is willer of the occurrence of everything whose occurrence He knows, and nothing happens in His dominion except what He wills to be; what God wills is, and what He does not will is not. And al-Ash'ari chose the statement in detail: that one should not restrict oneself to saying that God willed disbelief and fornication; rather I add a qualifier, so I say: He willed from the disbeliever disbelief as an acquisition from him, evil from him, blameworthy. And al-Nasafi says: Indeed, the view of al-Ash'ari is close to the view of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage discusses what falls under God's will. Al-Nasafi begins by stating that everything happens by God's will, including both obedience and sin. He then distinguishes: for acts of obedience, they are by God's will, desire, pleasure, love, command, decree, and power. For acts of disobedience, they are by God's will, desire, decree, and ordainment, but not by His command, pleasure, or love. This leads to a noted difference between Maturidis and Ash'aris: whether God's love and pleasure are synonymous with His will. The Maturidis say no, the Ash'aris say yes. Al-Nasafi then states that for the Maturidis, will corresponds to knowledge (God wills what He knows will happen), while for the Mu'tazila, will corresponds to command (God wills what He commands). He then cites 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan affirming that God wills everything He knows will occur. Finally, al-Nasafi presents al-Ash'ari's detailed position: to avoid simply saying God willed disbelief, one should add a qualifier that God willed the disbeliever's disbelief as an evil and blameworthy acquisition from him. Al-Nasafi comments that al-Ash'ari's view is close to that of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المشيئة
المحبة
الرضا
الطاعة
المعصية
الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills sin, then He must also love and command it.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that God wills sin but does not love or command it, preserving His perfect goodness.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi view presented by al-Nasafi, how are acts of disobedience related to God's will and pleasure?
A) They are by God's will but not by His command, pleasure, or love.
B) They are by God's will, command, and pleasure.
C) They are not by God's will at all.
D) They are by God's will only if they are not major sins.

Key Takeaway
Everything occurs by God's will, but only obedience is accompanied by His pleasure, love, and command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that God still wills it but does not love it? How does this reality affect the sincerity of your repentance?
?
Can you accept that your disobedience is part of God's decree while also knowing He hates it? Does this tension drive you closer to Him or cause you to despair?
?
If God does not love your sin, what does His love for you depend on? Are you satisfied that His love is conditional upon your obedience, or do you struggle with that?

The Maturidi-Ash'ari Difference on God's Will and Pleasure
Al-Nasafi discusses the Maturidi-Ash'ari disagreement on love/pleasure as will and explains the relationship of will to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi and Ash'ari positions on whether God's love and pleasure are synonymous with His will, and the relationship between will and knowledge.

Core Idea
Maturidis distinguish will from love; Ash'aris equate them.

Al-Nasafi presents a point of internal Sunni debate: are God's love and pleasure identical to His will or distinct? The Maturidis hold they are distinct, while the Ash'aris equate them. He then explains that for Maturidis, will aligns with knowledge, whereas the Mu'tazila align it with command. He concludes by citing al-Ash'ari's nuanced position, which al-Nasafi sees as close to the Maturidi view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation. Note the key question: Are love and pleasure the same as will?
2
Identify the two positions
Find where al-Nasafi states the Maturidi and Ash'ari answers. Then note his explanation about will corresponding to knowledge versus command.
Maturidis: love and pleasure are distinct. Ash'aris: they are the same. Will corresponds to knowledge (Maturidi) or command (Mu'tazila).
3
Examine al-Ash'ari's detailed statement
Read how al-Ash'ari avoids crude attribution by adding a qualifier. Then note al-Nasafi's comment that this is close to Maturidi.
Al-Ash'ari says God willed disbelief as an evil acquisition from the disbeliever.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول النسفي : إن كل شيء حدث بإرادة الله وعلى هذا فكل شيء يقع تحت إرادته تعالى. ثم يعرض آراء المعتزلة، ويقول : الأصل عندنا أن كل حادث حدث بإرادة الله تعالى على أي وصف كان، ثم ما كان في ذلك طاعة فهو بمشيئة الله، وإرادته، ورضاه، ومحبته، وأمره، وقضائه وقدرته، وما كان معصية فهو بمشيئة الله، وإرادته، وقضائه وقدره، وليس بأمره، ولا برضاه، ولا بمحبته. وهنا يذكر اختلافا بين الماتريدية، والأشاعرة وهو هل المحبة والرضا بمعنى الإرادة أم لا؟ يقول الماتريدية: لا، ويقول الأشاعرة: نعم. ويقول : إن الإرادة موافقة للعلم، وعند المعتزلة موافقة للأمر. ثم يعرض بعض الأقوال فيقول : قال عبد الله بن سعيد القطان: إن الله تعالى مريد حدوث كل ما علم حدوثه، ولا يكون في سلطانه إلا ما يريد كونه ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن. وقد اختار الأشعري القول عند التفصيل أن لا يقتصر على القول: أن الله سبحانه أراد الكفر، والزنا بل أزيد عليه قرينة، فأقول : أراد من الكافر الكفر كسبا له قبيحا منه، مذموما. ويقول النسفي : إن رأى الأشعري قريب من رأي أبي منصور الماتريدي،
Al-Nasafi says: Indeed, everything that occurs is by the will of God, and on this basis everything falls under His will, exalted. Then he presents the views of the Mu'tazila, and says: The principle with us is that every event occurs by the will of God Almighty, on whatever description it may be. Then what is in that an obedience (ta'a) is by God's will (mashi'a), His desire (irada), His pleasure (rida), His love (mahaba), His command (amr), His decree (qada') and His power (qudra). And what is a sin (ma'siya) is by God's will, His desire, His decree and His ordainment (qadr), and it is not by His command, nor by His pleasure, nor by His love. And here he mentions a disagreement between the Maturidis and the Ash'aris: namely, are love and pleasure synonymous with desire (irada) or not? The Maturidis say: No, and the Ash'aris say: Yes. And he says: Indeed, desire corresponds to knowledge ('ilm), while with the Mu'tazila it corresponds to command. Then he presents some statements and says: 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan said: Indeed, God Almighty is willer of the occurrence of everything whose occurrence He knows, and nothing happens in His dominion except what He wills to be; what God wills is, and what He does not will is not. And al-Ash'ari chose the statement in detail: that one should not restrict oneself to saying that God willed disbelief and fornication; rather I add a qualifier, so I say: He willed from the disbeliever disbelief as an acquisition from him, evil from him, blameworthy. And al-Nasafi says: Indeed, the view of al-Ash'ari is close to the view of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage discusses what falls under God's will. Al-Nasafi begins by stating that everything happens by God's will, including both obedience and sin. He then distinguishes: for acts of obedience, they are by God's will, desire, pleasure, love, command, decree, and power. For acts of disobedience, they are by God's will, desire, decree, and ordainment, but not by His command, pleasure, or love. This leads to a noted difference between Maturidis and Ash'aris: whether God's love and pleasure are synonymous with His will. The Maturidis say no, the Ash'aris say yes. Al-Nasafi then states that for the Maturidis, will corresponds to knowledge (God wills what He knows will happen), while for the Mu'tazila, will corresponds to command (God wills what He commands). He then cites 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan affirming that God wills everything He knows will occur. Finally, al-Nasafi presents al-Ash'ari's detailed position: to avoid simply saying God willed disbelief, one should add a qualifier that God willed the disbeliever's disbelief as an evil and blameworthy acquisition from him. Al-Nasafi comments that al-Ash'ari's view is close to that of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi.

Key Terms
المحبة
الرضا
الإرادة
العلم
الأمر
الكسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Sunnis believe God's love and will are identical.
Correction: Maturidis distinguish them, while Ash'aris equate them, but both affirm God wills all things.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the difference between Maturidis and Ash'aris regarding love and pleasure?
A) Both agree love and pleasure are synonymous with will.
B) Maturidis say they are not synonymous; Ash'aris say they are.
C) Ash'aris say they are not synonymous; Maturidis say they are.
D) Both deny they are synonymous.

Key Takeaway
Maturidis hold that God's love and pleasure are distinct from His will, while Ash'aris equate them; both agree that God wills all that occurs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God wills your every sin but does not love it, are you truly taking responsibility for your actions, or do you use His decree as a shield from guilt? When you sin, do you feel the weight of displeasing Him, or do you presume indifference because He willed it?
?
Al-Ash'ari qualifies how God wills disbelief—as an evil acquisition from the disbeliever. Have you ever spoken carelessly about God's will regarding your own wrongs, as if He simply wanted you to sin without the moral gravity? Will you from now on add the same reverence and precision when speaking of His will?
?
The Maturidis separate God's love from His will. Does your heart feel secure in God's love for you despite your failures, or do you mistakenly think His will for your trials means He has abandoned you? How can this distinction quiet your fears and deepen your trust?

Everything Occurs by God's Will
Nasafi asserts the universal scope of divine will over all events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi principle that every contingent event occurs by the will of Allah, and why this is foundational for distinguishing between obedience and disobedience.

Core Idea
Everything that happens is willed by Allah — even the details of human actions.

Nasafi begins by establishing a foundational principle: no event escapes Allah's will. This is a core Maturidi position that rejects the Mu'tazilite view that some actions occur without divine will. Understanding this sets the stage for a nuanced discussion of obedience and disobedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening assertion
Read the Arabic passage from the heading through 'on whatever description it may be'. Notice the universal scope: 'every contingent event'.
The heading asks what is valid to enter under God's will — the answer is everything.
2
Contrast with Mu'tazila
Nasafi mentions he will present the views of the Mu'tazila. This contrast highlights that the Maturidi position is deliberately broad.
The Mu'tazila held that some actions, especially human acts of disobedience, are not willed by God.
3
Reflect on 'in whatever description'
The phrase 'in whatever description it may be' is key. It means the moral quality (obedience/disobedience) does not remove an action from God's will.
This prepares us for the next lesson's distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يصح دخوله تحت إرادة الله: يقول النسفي : إن كل شيء حدث بإرادة الله وعلى هذا فكل شيء يقع تحت إرادته تعالى. ثم يعرض آراء المعتزلة، ويقول : الأصل عندنا أن كل حادث حدث بإرادة الله تعالى على أي وصف كان، ثم ما كان في ذلك طاعة فهو بمشيئة الله، وإرادته، ورضاه، ومحبته، وأمره، وقضائه وقدرته، وما كان معصية فهو بمشيئة الله، وإرادته، وقضائه وقدره، وليس بأمره، ولا برضاه، ولا بمحبته.
What is valid to enter under the will of Allah: Al-Nasafi says: Indeed, everything that occurs occurs by the will of Allah, and upon this, everything falls under His will, exalted. Then he presents the views of the Mu'tazila, and says: The principle with us is that every contingent event occurs by the will of Allah Almighty in whatever description it may be. Then whatever in that is obedience, it is by the will of Allah, His desire (irada), His pleasure (rida), His love (mahabbah), His command (amr), His decree (qada'), and His power (qudra). And whatever is disobedience, it is by the will of Allah, His desire, His decree, and His power, but not by His command, nor His pleasure, nor His love.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage establishes the foundational Maturidi principle that all events are under God's will. It begins with a heading: 'What is valid to enter under the will of Allah.' Al-Nasafi affirms that every occurrence is by God's will. He then contrasts this with the Mu'tazila position. Next, he details the principle: all contingent events happen by God's will regardless of their description. However, within that, acts of obedience are additionally associated with God's will, desire, pleasure, love, command, decree, and power. Acts of disobedience, while also occurring by God's will, desire, decree, and power, are not associated with His command, pleasure, or love. This distinction is crucial for understanding the Maturidi doctrine that God wills all actions but commands only good.

Key Terms
إرادة الله
مشيئة الله
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi principle stated by Nasafi, which of the following is true?
A) Only obedient actions occur by God's will.
B) Disobedient actions occur by God's will but not by His decree.
C) Every contingent event occurs by God's will regardless of its description.
D) Only events that are good occur by God's will.

Key Takeaway
Every event, without exception, falls under the will of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you see evil in the world, do you doubt Allah's wisdom or power? How can the truth that everything—including evil—falls under His will actually strengthen your trust in Him rather than weaken it? Make a concrete decision to change your response to the next trial you face.

Obedience vs Disobedience in Divine Attributes
Nasafi enumerates the divine attributes associated with obedience and those withheld from disobedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidis distinguish actions of obedience from actions of disobedience in terms of divine attributes: while both are willed by Allah, only obedience is commanded, loved, and pleased by Him.

Core Idea
Obedience is commanded and loved; disobedience is willed but not loved.

Having established that all events are by God's will, Nasafi now clarifies a critical nuance: not everything willed by God is also commanded, loved, or pleasing to Him. This distinction protects God's perfection while affirming His absolute will.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'ثم ما كان في ذلك طاعة' to the end. Notice the two lists.
2
Identify the attributes for obedience
List the attributes mentioned for acts of obedience: will, desire, pleasure, love, command, decree, power.
3
Identify the attributes for disobedience
List the attributes for disobedience: will, desire, decree, power. Notice the missing ones: command, pleasure, love.
The absence of these three is significant.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Ask why God would will disobedience but not command or love it. Think about divine wisdom and justice.
This distinction safeguards God from being associated with evil.

Debate on al-Aslah: The Most Beneficial for Creation
Nasafi presents the Ahl al-Haqq and Mu'tazila positions on the obligation of al-aslah on Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different theological positions on whether Allah is obligated to create the most beneficial (al-aslah) for His creation, and the key terms used in this debate.

Core Idea
Allah is not obligated to create the most beneficial; His acts are by favor, not obligation.

The doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial) was a major point of contention between the Mu'tazila and the Ahl al-Haqq. The Mu'tazila argued that it is obligatory upon Allah to do what is most beneficial for His creation, while the Ahl al-Haqq maintained that Allah's actions are free from any external obligation. This passage from Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla presents the two positions and introduces key terms like lutf (kindness) and tafaddul (favor).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the structure of different positions come through.
Notice that the passage names three groups: Ahl al-Haqq, majority Mu'tazila, and Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir.
2
Identify the core argument of Ahl al-Haqq
Find what Ahl al-Haqq say about Allah's power to grant lutf and whether He is obligated to do so.
They affirm His power but deny obligation.
3
Contrast with the Mu'tazila position
Note how the Mu'tazila deny the existence of such a lutf in Allah's power, and what they consider obligatory.
They argue that if it were in His power, withholding it would be foolish.
4
Understand Bishr's position and its consequence
Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir held a view close to Ahl al-Haqq, leading the Mu'tazila to declare him a disbeliever.
This shows the sectarian boundary.
5
Distinguish the definitions of al-aslah
Notice how Baghdad and Basran Mu'tazila defined the term differently.
Baghdad: wisdom/planning; Basran: benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الأصلح: يقول أهل الحق : إن في مقدور الله تعالى لطفا، لو فعل ذلك بالكفار لآمنوا اختيارا، وإذا لم يعطهم لم يكن بخيلا ولا ظالما، وإعطاء اللطف منه تعالى يكون تفضلا ولا يجب عليه شيء. أما جمهور المعتزلة فإنهم يدعون أنه ليس في مقدور الله تعالى لطف لو فعل بالكفار لأمنوا ، ولو كان ذلك في مقدوره ولم يفعل لكان سفيها بخيلا، وغاية ما يقدر عليه مما به صلاح الخلق واجب عليه. أما بشر بن المعتمر فيقول بما هو قريب من أهل الحق ولهذا كفرته معتزلة ثم يعرف النسفي الأصلح عند معتزلة بغداد بأنه الأصلح في الحكمة والتدبير، وعند بعض البصريين الصلاح هو النفع والأصلح هو الأنفع.
The discussion on al-aslah (the most beneficial): The people of truth say: Indeed, Allah has the power to do a kindness (lutf); if He did it to the disbelievers, they would believe voluntarily, and if He does not give it to them, He is neither miserly nor unjust. Giving the kindness from Him is a favor (tafaddul) and nothing is obligatory upon Him. As for the majority of the Mu'tazila, they claim that there is no kindness within Allah's power such that if He did it to the disbelievers they would believe; and if that were in His power and He did not do it, He would be foolish and miserly. The utmost that He is capable of, that which brings about the good of creation, is obligatory upon Him. As for Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, he says something close to the people of truth, and for this the Mu'tazila declared him a disbeliever. Then al-Nasafi defines al-aslah according to the Mu'tazila of Baghdad as that which is most beneficial in terms of wisdom and planning, and according to some of the Basrans, righteousness (al-salah) is benefit and the most beneficial (al-aslah) is the most beneficial.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a classification and comparison of different theological positions on the doctrine of al-aslah (the most beneficial). It begins by stating the Ahl al-Haqq position: Allah has the ability to perform a lutf (kindness) that would cause disbelievers to voluntarily believe, but He is not obligated to do so; if He withholds it, that is not injustice. Then it presents the dominant Mu'tazila view: they deny that any such lutf is in Allah's power; if it were and He withheld it, He would be foolish and miserly. They hold that whatever is necessary for the good of creation is obligatory upon Him. Then it mentions Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir who held a view similar to Ahl al-Haqq, for which the Mu'tazila declared him an unbeliever. Finally, it defines the term according to the Baghdad school of Mu'tazila (aslah in terms of wisdom and planning) and the Basran school (salah as benefit, aslah as more beneficial).

Key Terms
الأصلح
اللطف
التفضل
الوجوب
الحكمة والتدبير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ahl al-Haqq deny Allah's ability to do good for creation.
Correction: Ahl al-Haqq affirm that Allah is able to grant kindness (lutf) that would bring people to faith, but they deny that He is obligated to do so.

Checkpoint
According to the Ahl al-Haqq, what is the status of giving lutf (kindness) to disbelievers?
A) It is obligatory upon Allah.
B) It is a favor (tafaddul) and not obligatory.
C) It is impossible for Allah.
D) It is necessary for the good of creation.

Key Takeaway
The debate over al-aslah turns on whether Allah is obligated to create the most beneficial; Ahl al-Haqq affirm His freedom from obligation, while Mu'tazila argue for obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or loss, do you ever quietly blame Allah for not giving you what you think is best? How does realizing that His gifts are favors, not obligations, change your heart towards Him in that moment? Will you practice gratitude for the favors you have instead of resentment for what you lack?
?
If you truly believe that nothing is obligatory upon Allah, does that make you feel more secure in His mercy or more anxious about your fate? How does this teaching affect your daily trust in Allah's decisions for your life? Will you this week surrender one disappointment to Him as His unowed favor?
?
The Mu'tazila thought Allah would be 'foolish and miserly' if He withheld kindness. Are there areas in your life where you judge Allah's wisdom because He hasn't given you what you consider good? Will you repent from measuring Allah's actions by your own standards and instead submit to His wisdom?

Proof from Ijma: The Argument from Prayer
Nasafi uses the consensus on prayer for help to argue against the Mu'tazila position on al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus on prayer for help in obedience and protection from sin refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-aslah (that Allah must always do what is most beneficial for His servants).

Core Idea
The universal practice of supplication disproves any obligation upon Allah.

Muslims unanimously pray to Allah for help in doing good and avoiding sin. But what does this prayer imply about Allah's actions? Imam Nasafi uses this simple practice to dismantle the Mu'tazila claim that Allah is obligated to do the most beneficial for His servants. The argument hinges on a logical breakdown of what it means if Allah answers or withholds the requested help.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical structure: the author breaks the matter into possibilities.
The passage is concise; pay attention to each conditional.
2
Identify the two main branches
The argument splits: either Allah gives what is asked, or He does not. Trace each branch and its consequences.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila refutation
Focus on the final branch where it is permissible for Allah not to give. Why does this nullify the Mu'tazila position?
The key is that the servant's action then shares agency with Allah.
4
Reflect on the disbelief punishment
Notice how the author declares disbelief on anyone who claims Allah is obligated to give help and that withholding would be injustice.
This shows the seriousness of the theological error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الدليل من الإجماع فقد أجمع أهل الأديان والمسلمون على الدعاء الله تعالى وطلب المعونة للطاعات والعصمة عن المعاصي، والأمر هنا لا يخلو كما يقول النسفي: إما أن يكون ما سألوا من المعونة آتاهم الله، أو كان لم يؤتهم؟ فإن كان آتاهم، فسؤالهم سفه، جل ربنا أن يأمر عباده بالاشتغال بما هو سفه. وإن كان لم يؤتهم - لا يخلو إما أن يجوز له أن لا يؤتيهم، أو كان لا يجوز. فإن كان الثاني بل يجب عليه ذلك، كان منحه ظالما مانعا حقا مستحقا، وكان السؤال والدعاء كأنهم قالوا: اللهم لا تظلمنا بمنع حقنا المستحق عليك. ومن يقول بذلك أو يجيز القول به فهو كافر . وإن كان الأول: فقد بطل مذهب المعتزلة لأن الله يكون بذلك شريكا في فعل العبد
As for the proof from consensus, all adherents of religions and Muslims have agreed on supplicating Allah and asking for help in acts of obedience and protection from sins. The matter here is not free, as al-Nasafi says: either what they asked for of help Allah gave them, or He did not give them? If He gave them, then their asking is foolishness — exalted is our Lord above commanding His servants to occupy themselves with what is foolish. And if He did not give them — it is not free from either that it is permissible for Him not to give them, or it is not permissible. If the second, rather it is obligatory upon Him, then His withholding would be unjust, preventing a due right, and the asking and supplication would be as if they said: 'O Allah, do not wrong us by preventing our right that is due upon You.' Whoever says that or allows saying it is a disbeliever. And if the first [i.e., it is permissible for Him not to give], then the Mu'tazila doctrine is nullified because Allah would then be a partner in the servant's action.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a proof from ijma' (consensus) against the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-aslah. The author begins by stating the universal consensus on praying to Allah for help in obedience and protection from sin. He then breaks the argument into logical possibilities. If Allah always grants the help asked, then prayer is foolish, which cannot be attributed to Allah's command. If He sometimes does not grant it, it is either permissible for Him to withhold or obligatory to give. If it is obligatory, then withholding would be unjust, and the prayer would be demanding a right, which implies accusing Allah of potential injustice — that is disbelief. If it is permissible to withhold, then the Mu'tazila position collapses because Allah shares agency with the servant (since the servant's action is not entirely created by Allah). This refutes the Mu'tazila claim that Allah must always do the most beneficial.

Key Terms
الإجماع
الدعاء
المعونة
الحق المستحق
الظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if Muslims pray for help, it means Allah is obligated to respond and grant it.
Correction: The passage shows that if Allah were obligated, then withholding would be injustice, which is impossible for Allah. Rather, prayer is an act of worship, not a demand.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if it is obligatory for Allah to give the help asked in prayer?
A) Prayer is foolishness.
B) Allah would be unjust if He withholds, and that belief amounts to disbelief.
C) The Mu'tazila doctrine is confirmed.
D) The servant's action is entirely from Allah.

Key Takeaway
The universal practice of supplication logically disproves both the notion that Allah is obligated to grant help and the Mu'tazila doctrine that Allah must always do the most beneficial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you secretly expect Allah to grant your request because you think He owes you something? How does this lesson expose that assumption as a potential form of kufr?
?
This argument shows that if Allah always granted help, supplication would be foolish. Have you ever prayed mechanically, as if it were a formula to get results? How can you transform your du'a into a genuine act of submission, knowing that Allah may withhold for a wisdom you do not see?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is never obligated to answer your prayers, then what does that mean for your attitude when He does not give you what you ask? Are you willing to accept His withholding as just and wise, or do you harbor a sense of entitlement that contradicts the very foundation of this proof?

The Dilemma of Supplication
Nasafi constructs a dilemma from consensus on supplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the universal practice of supplication to refute the Mu'tazila doctrine that God is obliged to grant the most beneficial.

Core Idea
Supplication implies that God can choose to give or withhold—refuting the Mu'tazila's compulsion.

One of the most powerful arguments in kalam is drawing attention to what believers actually do. Nasafi points to the universal practice of supplication—practiced by Muslims and non-Muslims alike—and asks a simple question: if asking for help makes sense, what does that imply about God’s freedom and our responsibility? In this lesson, you will see how a simple act of worship becomes a theological proof.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the consensus premise
The passage starts by stating that all religious people agree on supplication. This is the foundation of the argument.
Consensus (ijma') is a powerful source of proof in Islamic theology.
2
Understand the first alternative
If God always grants what is asked, then supplication is foolish. This shows that cannot be the case.
Nasafi uses reductio ad absurdum.
3
Analyze the second alternative
If God does not grant it, then either He may withhold or it is obligatory to grant. The latter leads to injustice, which is impossible.
Nasafi concludes that the Mu'tazila position leads to absurd consequences.
4
Draw the conclusion
Nasafi then exposes the Mu'tazila's underlying view that God has no choice and is compelled to do the most beneficial. This denies God’s bounty and gratitude.
Thus, consensus on supplication refutes the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الدليل من الإجماع فقد أجمع أهل الأديان والمسلمون على الدعاء الله تعالى وطلب المعونة للطاعات والعصمة عن المعاصي، والأمر هنا لا يخلو كما يقول النسفي: إما أن يكون ما سألوا من المعونة آتاهم الله، أو كان لم يؤتهم؟ فإن كان آتاهم، فسؤالهم سفه، جل ربنا أن يأمر عباده بالاشتغال بما هو سفه. وإن كان لم يؤتهم - لا يخلو إما أن يجوز له أن لا يؤتيهم، أو كان لا يجوز. فإن كان الثاني بل يجب عليه ذلك، كان منحه ظالما مانعا حقا مستحقا، وكان السؤال والدعاء كأنهم قالوا: اللهم لا تظلمنا بمنع حقنا المستحق عليك. ومن يقول بذلك أو يجيز القول به فهو كافر . وإن كان الأول: فقد بطل مذهب المعتزلة لأن الله يكون بذلك شريكا في فعل العبد وفي النهاية يقول النسفي : إن حاصل هذه المسألة عند المعتزلة أن الله تعالى لا مشيئة له في فعله ولا اختيار بل هو مجبور على فعل ما هو الأصلح للعباد، وليس الله في رأيهم بمحسن ولا متفضل لأنه في فعله يقضي حقا مستحقا عليه على وجه يصير منعه ظالما. تعالى الله عما يقولون، فهم بذلك يكذبونه في قوله تعالى: وَاللَّهُ ذُو الْفَضْلِ الْعَظِيمِ ﴾ [آل عمران : ٧٤] وهو بذلك غير مستوجب شكرا لأنه يقضي حق يحدون من قدرة الله لأنهم يجعلونه غير قادر على فعل أصلح وهم أيضا وكلامهم في الصلاح والأصلح يؤدي إلى ضلالات أكثر وأكثر .. تعالى ربنا عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا.
As for the evidence from consensus: the people of religions and Muslims have agreed upon supplication to Allah Almighty and asking for assistance in acts of obedience and protection from sins. This matter, as al-Nasafi says, is not free: either what they asked of assistance was given to them by Allah, or it was not given. If He gave it, then their request is foolishness—far exalted is our Lord above commanding His servants to occupy themselves with foolishness. If He did not give it—it is not free from either it being permissible for Him not to give it, or it being not permissible. If the second, and it is incumbent upon Him, then His giving would be unjust, preventing a rightful right, and the request and supplication would be as if they said: 'O Allah, do not wrong us by preventing our rightful right due from You.' Whoever says that or permits saying it is an unbeliever. If the first, then the school of the Mu'tazila is invalidated, because Allah would thereby be a partner in the action of the servant. In conclusion, al-Nasafi says: the outcome of this issue according to the Mu'tazila is that Allah Almighty has no will in His action and no choice, but rather He is compelled to do what is most beneficial for the servants. In their view, Allah is not a benefactor nor gracious, because in His action He fulfills a right incumbent upon Him in a way that making His prevention becomes unjust. Exalted is Allah above what they say; thus they deny His saying: 'And Allah is the Possessor of great bounty' (Qur'an 3:74). And He is thereby not deserving of thanks because He fulfills a right. They restrict the power of Allah because they make Him incapable of doing what is more beneficial, and they also... And their speech about good and what is more beneficial leads to more and more misguidance. Exalted is our Lord above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is an argument from consensus (ijma') refuting the Mu'tazila's doctrine of divine assistance (lutf) and the obligation of al-aslah (the most beneficial).

The argument proceeds as follows:
1. There is consensus among all religious communities and Muslims on supplicating to Allah and asking for His help in obedience and protection from sins.
2. Al-Nasafi (the author) frames a dilemma: either Allah grants what is asked, or He does not.
   - If He grants it, then the request is foolish, because one is asking for what is already given. It would be absurd for Allah to command such foolishness.
   - If He does not grant it, then either it is permissible for Him to withhold it or it is not permissible (i.e., obligatory to grant).
3. If it were obligatory for Allah to grant the request, then withholding it would be injustice (zulm), and the supplicants would be asking Allah not to wrong them by withholding a right. Anyone who says that is an unbeliever (kafir).
4. If it is permissible for Him to withhold (the first alternative), then the Mu'tazila's position is invalidated because it implies that Allah is a partner in the servant's action? Actually, the text says 'because Allah would thereby be a partner in the action of the servant'—meaning that if Allah had the power to grant assistance but does not, then the servant's action is independent of Allah, which contradicts the Mu'tazila's own view? This part is somewhat condensed.
5. Al-Nasafi then summarizes the Mu'tazila's position: they deny that Allah has a will (mashi'a) or choice (ikhtiyar) in His actions, claiming He is compelled to do what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants. Consequently, they deny that Allah is a benefactor (muhsin) or gracious (mutafaddil) because He is only fulfilling an obligation.
6. Al-Nasafi argues that this makes Allah undeserving of thanks and restricts His power, as they claim He cannot do anything more beneficial than what He does.
7. The passage ends with declaring that such speech leads to misguidance and exalting Allah above what they say.

Throughout, the commentary follows the logical progression of the dilemma and its implications.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijma')
لطف (lutf)
صلاح (salah)
أصلح (aslah)
مشيئة (mashi'a)
اختيار (ikhtiyar)
محسن (muhsin)
متفضل (mutafaddil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage argues that God is unjust if He does not grant every request.
Correction: The passage actually refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God must always grant the most beneficial; it does not assert that God is unjust for not granting requests.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what would be the consequence if it were obligatory for Allah to grant every request for assistance?
A) Allah would be unjust if He withheld it.
B) The request would be foolish.
C) The Mu'tazila's doctrine would be valid.
D) Allah would be a partner in the servant's action.

Key Takeaway
The universal practice of supplication implies that God can choose to grant or withhold assistance, refuting the Mu'tazila doctrine that God is compelled to grant the most beneficial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you truly believe that Allah has absolute freedom to answer or withhold, or do you sometimes feel He 'owes' you an answer? How does this lesson challenge your expectation?
?
If you knew that Allah was obligated to grant your requests, how would your du'a change? Would it still be humble? This passage says that would make du'a a demand, not a plea. Examine your heart: do you ever make du'a with a sense of entitlement?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending Allah's justice, but Nasafi shows they ended up denying His grace. Where in your own thinking have you tried to 'protect' Allah from criticism by imposing rules on Him? What does this teach you about trust in His perfect wisdom?

Defining Qadim and Muhdath
Nasafi defines qadim and muhdath and warns of metaphorical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of qadim (eternal) and muhdath (originated) as given by al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila, and the metaphorical use of 'qadim'.

Core Idea
The eternal has no beginning; the originated has a beginning.

Imam Nasafi begins by establishing the foundational definitions needed for discussing whether the world is eternal or originated. He presents the definition of qadim given by al-Ash'ari, which is also accepted by the Mu'tazila, and then gives multiple expressions for muhdath, all pointing to the same meaning. He also clarifies that the term 'qadim' can be used metaphorically for things that are merely ancient, which is not the intended sense in theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on definitions
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation to get the flow.
2
Compare the definitions of qadim
Notice that al-Ash'ari defines qadim as 'that which has no beginning' and that the Mu'tazila accept this definition despite their view on attributes.
The Mu'tazila do not describe God with attributes beyond the essence, yet they accept this definition.
3
Understand the definitions of muhdath
Recognize that multiple expressions (has a beginning, has a first, is begun, comes after the eternal) all convey one meaning.
This shows the richness of Arabic expression.
4
Notice the metaphorical usage warning
The author warns that 'qadim' can be used for relatively ancient things, like an old building, but this is not the intended meaning in theological discussions.
This prevents a common confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد (۸) ذلك يحتاج إلى معرفة حد القديم وحد (۹) المحدث فنقول : القديم ما لا أول لوجوده وهذا تحديد الأشعري، وهو المختار عند المعتزلة أيضا، لما أنه تعالى موصوف به، وعندهم لا يوصف هو بما هو معنى وراء الذات. وقال عبد الله بن سعيد القطان (١٠) : القديم من له قدم ، وينشأ عن اختلاف التحديد (۱۱) بين عبد الله بن سعيد وبين الأشعري خلاف في أن صفات الله تعالى هل توصف بأنها قديمة؟ فالأشعري يصفها بذلك لما أنه لا أول لوجودها، وعبد الله بن سعيد مع قدماء أصحابنا يمتنعون عن ذلك، لاستحالة قيام القدم وهو صفة بما وراءها من الصفات، إذ الصفة لا تقوم بها صفة، وإن كانوا قالوا: لا ابتداء لوجودها ، فكأن هذا اختلاف في العبارة. وأما المحدث فهو ما لوجوده ابتداء ويقال ما لوجوده أول ، وقيل : هو المبتدأ في الوجود، وقيل: هو ما تأخر بوجوده عن الأزلي. وهذه العبارات كلها تنبئ (۱) عن معنى واحد قد يطلق اسم القديم على ما لوجوده ابتداء، فيقال : هذا بناء قديم وشيخ قديم، يراد به التقدم في الوجود على غيره بشرط المبالغة، وهذا النوع ليس بمراد في التكلم في العالم أنه قديم أم محدث، والله الموفق.
And after (8) that, one needs to know the definition of the eternal (al-qadīm) and the definition (9) of the originated (al-muḥdath). So we say: The eternal is that which has no beginning to its existence—this is the determination of al-Ashʿarī, and it is also the chosen view of the Muʿtazila, because He (Exalted) is described by it, and according to them He is not described by something that is a meaning beyond the essence. And ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd al-Qaṭṭān (10) said: The eternal is that which has pre-eternity (qidam). And arising from the difference in determination (11) between ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd and al-Ashʿarī is a disagreement concerning whether the attributes of God (Exalted) are described as eternal? Al-Ashʿarī describes them as such because there is no beginning to their existence, and ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd along with the early scholars of our companions refrain from that, due to the impossibility of pre-eternity (al-qidam)—which is an attribute—subsisting in what is beyond it from among the attributes, for an attribute does not subsist in an attribute, even though they said: there is no beginning to its existence. So this seems to be a difference in expression. As for the originated (al-muḥdath), it is that whose existence has a beginning, and it is said: that whose existence has a first, and it is said: it is the one whose existence is begun, and it is said: it is that which in its existence comes after the eternal. And all these expressions indicate (1) one meaning. Sometimes the name 'eternal' is applied to that whose existence has a beginning, so it is said: this is an ancient (qadīm) building and an ancient (qadīm) old man, meaning precedence in existence over others with a condition of exaggeration. But this type is not intended when speaking about whether the world is eternal or originated. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage defines the two key terms qadīm (eternal) and muḥdath (originated), and then discusses a disagreement about applying 'qadīm' to God's attributes. It begins by stating that after the previous discussion, one needs to know these definitions. The definition of qadīm is 'that which has no beginning to its existence'—this is the definition of al-Ashʿarī, also accepted by the Muʿtazila because God is described by it, though the Muʿtazila do not describe Him by a meaning beyond the essence. Then ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd al-Qaṭṭān defines qadīm as 'that which has pre-eternity (qidam)'. This definitional difference leads to a substantive disagreement: can God's attributes be described as qadīm? Al-Ashʿarī says yes, because they have no beginning. But ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd and the early Māturīdī scholars refrain, because it is impossible for qidam (an attribute) to subsist in another attribute; an attribute does not subsist in an attribute. However, they agree that the attributes have no beginning, so the disagreement is merely terminological. Next, the author gives multiple definitions of muḥdath: that whose existence has a beginning, a first, is begun, or comes after the eternal. All these amount to one meaning. Finally, a clarification: the word 'qadīm' can be used metaphorically for something with a beginning but relatively ancient, like an old building or old man, but this is not the intended sense when discussing whether the world is eternal or originated.

Key Terms
قديم (eternal)
محدث (originated)
قدم (pre-eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'qadim' is only used to mean absolutely eternal in all contexts.
Correction: The passage explains that 'qadim' can be used metaphorically for something relatively ancient, like an old building, but this is not the intended meaning when discussing whether the world is eternal or originated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of qadim given by al-Ash'ari?
A) That which has no beginning to its existence.
B) That which has pre-eternity (qidam).
C) That which has a beginning.
D) That which comes after the eternal.

Key Takeaway
The eternal (qadim) is defined as that which has no beginning, while the originated (muhdath) has a beginning, and the term 'qadim' can be used metaphorically for things that are merely ancient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God alone is truly qadim — without beginning — then everything you cling to in this world is muhdath, destined to pass. Which possessions, relationships, or ambitions do you subconsciously treat as though they will never end? Write down one and ask yourself: does my attachment to this thing reveal a deeper forgetfulness of God's eternity? Make a concrete decision today to loosen your grip on it.
?
The passage warns that we can use 'qadim' loosely for ancient things. In your prayer or worship, do you ever treat God's commands as 'ancient' in a dismissive sense — outdated or irrelevant — rather than as the speech of the Eternal One? If so, what would it mean for you to approach one command this week as truly originating from the One who has no beginning?

Disagreement on Calling Attributes Qadim
Nasafi records the dispute on attributing qadim to God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement between al-Ash'ari and ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd al-Qaṭṭān regarding whether God's attributes are described as qadim, and why the author considers it a terminological difference.

Core Idea
The attributes have no beginning, but calling them qadim is disputed due to a definitional nuance.

After defining qadim and muhdath, Imam Nasafi presents a specific dispute arising from a difference in the definition of qadim. Al-Ash'ari, who defines qadim as 'that which has no beginning,' applies the term to God's attributes. Al-Qattan, who defines qadim as 'that which has pre-eternity (qidam),' refrains because it would imply an attribute (qidam) subsisting in another attribute, which is impossible. However, both agree the attributes have no beginning, so the disagreement is merely verbal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the disagreement
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: definition, dispute, positions, resolution.
2
Understand al-Ash'ari's position
Al-Ash'ari calls attributes qadim because they have no beginning, based on his definition.
He follows the definition he gave earlier.
3
Understand al-Qattan's position
Al-Qattan refrains because qidam (pre-eternity) is an attribute and cannot subsist in another attribute.
This is based on his definition of qadim as having qidam.
4
Grasp the author's resolution
The author says both sides agree the attributes have no beginning, so the disagreement is merely a difference in expression.
This shows the importance of understanding definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد (۸) ذلك يحتاج إلى معرفة حد القديم وحد (۹) المحدث فنقول : القديم ما لا أول لوجوده وهذا تحديد الأشعري، وهو المختار عند المعتزلة أيضا، لما أنه تعالى موصوف به، وعندهم لا يوصف هو بما هو معنى وراء الذات. وقال عبد الله بن سعيد القطان (١٠) : القديم من له قدم ، وينشأ عن اختلاف التحديد (۱۱) بين عبد الله بن سعيد وبين الأشعري خلاف في أن صفات الله تعالى هل توصف بأنها قديمة؟ فالأشعري يصفها بذلك لما أنه لا أول لوجودها، وعبد الله بن سعيد مع قدماء أصحابنا يمتنعون عن ذلك، لاستحالة قيام القدم وهو صفة بما وراءها من الصفات، إذ الصفة لا تقوم بها صفة، وإن كانوا قالوا: لا ابتداء لوجودها ، فكأن هذا اختلاف في العبارة. وأما المحدث فهو ما لوجوده ابتداء ويقال ما لوجوده أول ، وقيل : هو المبتدأ في الوجود، وقيل: هو ما تأخر بوجوده عن الأزلي. وهذه العبارات كلها تنبئ (۱) عن معنى واحد قد يطلق اسم القديم على ما لوجوده ابتداء، فيقال : هذا بناء قديم وشيخ قديم، يراد به التقدم في الوجود على غيره بشرط المبالغة، وهذا النوع ليس بمراد في التكلم في العالم أنه قديم أم محدث، والله الموفق.
And after (8) that, one needs to know the definition of the eternal (al-qadīm) and the definition (9) of the originated (al-muḥdath). So we say: The eternal is that which has no beginning to its existence—this is the determination of al-Ashʿarī, and it is also the chosen view of the Muʿtazila, because He (Exalted) is described by it, and according to them He is not described by something that is a meaning beyond the essence. And ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd al-Qaṭṭān (10) said: The eternal is that which has pre-eternity (qidam). And arising from the difference in determination (11) between ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd and al-Ashʿarī is a disagreement concerning whether the attributes of God (Exalted) are described as eternal? Al-Ashʿarī describes them as such because there is no beginning to their existence, and ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd along with the early scholars of our companions refrain from that, due to the impossibility of pre-eternity (al-qidam)—which is an attribute—subsisting in what is beyond it from among the attributes, for an attribute does not subsist in an attribute, even though they said: there is no beginning to its existence. So this seems to be a difference in expression. As for the originated (al-muḥdath), it is that whose existence has a beginning, and it is said: that whose existence has a first, and it is said: it is the one whose existence is begun, and it is said: it is that which in its existence comes after the eternal. And all these expressions indicate (1) one meaning. Sometimes the name 'eternal' is applied to that whose existence has a beginning, so it is said: this is an ancient (qadīm) building and an ancient (qadīm) old man, meaning precedence in existence over others with a condition of exaggeration. But this type is not intended when speaking about whether the world is eternal or originated. And God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage defines the two key terms qadīm (eternal) and muḥdath (originated), and then discusses a disagreement about applying 'qadīm' to God's attributes. It begins by stating that after the previous discussion, one needs to know these definitions. The definition of qadīm is 'that which has no beginning to its existence'—this is the definition of al-Ashʿarī, also accepted by the Muʿtazila because God is described by it, though the Muʿtazila do not describe Him by a meaning beyond the essence. Then ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd al-Qaṭṭān defines qadīm as 'that which has pre-eternity (qidam)'. This definitional difference leads to a substantive disagreement: can God's attributes be described as qadīm? Al-Ashʿarī says yes, because they have no beginning. But ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd and the early Māturīdī scholars refrain, because it is impossible for qidam (an attribute) to subsist in another attribute; an attribute does not subsist in an attribute. However, they agree that the attributes have no beginning, so the disagreement is merely terminological. Next, the author gives multiple definitions of muḥdath: that whose existence has a beginning, a first, is begun, or comes after the eternal. All these amount to one meaning. Finally, a clarification: the word 'qadīm' can be used metaphorically for something with a beginning but relatively ancient, like an old building or old man, but this is not the intended sense when discussing whether the world is eternal or originated.

Key Terms
قديم (eternal)
قدم (pre-eternity)
صفات (attributes)
ذات (essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari and al-Qattan have a fundamental theological disagreement on the attributes.
Correction: The author states that both agree the attributes have no beginning; the disagreement is only in expression due to differing definitions of qadim.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qattan, why should we not call God's attributes qadim?
A) Because they have a beginning.
B) Because qidam is an attribute and cannot subsist in another attribute.
C) Because the Mu'tazila forbid it.
D) Because the Quran does not use that term.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over calling attributes qadim is rooted in differing definitions of qadim, but both sides agree the attributes have no beginning, making it a terminological issue.

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
The Disagreement on Describing Attributes as Qadīm
Discusses the Ash'ari-Maturidi disagreement on calling attributes qadīm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key disagreement between al-Ashʿarī and the early Māturīdīs regarding whether God's attributes can be described as eternal (qadīm), and the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
The disagreement is about language, not doctrine; both agree attributes have no beginning.

The early Māturīdīs and al-Ashʿarī agreed on the eternity of God's attributes but differed on whether the term 'qadīm' could be applied to them. This lesson explores the subtle logical argument that led to a verbal difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'فكأن هذا اختلاف في العبارة' which indicates the verbal nature of the disagreement.
2
Identify the two positions and their justifications
Find al-Ashʿarī's position (he describes attributes as qadīm because they have no beginning) and the Māturīdī position (refrain because an attribute cannot subsist in another attribute).
The Māturīdī argument is based on a logical principle: an attribute cannot be described by another attribute.
3
Understand why this is considered a verbal disagreement
Note that the Māturīdīs also affirm that the attributes have no beginning. The difference is only in whether to use the term 'qadīm' for attributes.
This shows the importance of precision in theological language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبين الأشعري خلاف في أن صفات الله تعالى هل توصف بأنها قديمة؟ فالأشعري يصفها بذلك لما أنه لا أول لوجودها، وعبد الله بن سعيد مع قدماء أصحابنا يمتنعون عن ذلك، لاستحالة قيام القدم وهو صفة بما وراءها من الصفات، إذ الصفة لا تقوم بها صفة، وإن كانوا قالوا: لا ابتداء لوجودها، فكأن هذا اختلاف في العبارة. وأما المحدث فهو ما لوجوده ابتداء ويقال ما لوجوده أول، وقيل: هو المبتدأ في الوجود، وقيل: هو ما تأخر بوجوده عن الأزلي. وهذه العبارات كلها تنبئ عن معنى واحد قد يطلق اسم القديم على ما لوجوده ابتداء، فيقال: هذا بناء قديم وشيخ قديم، يراد به التقدم في الوجود على غيره بشرط المبالغة، وهذا النوع ليس بمراد في التكلم في العالم أنه قديم أم محدث، والله الموفق.
And between al-Ashʿarī and [the Māturīdīs] there is a disagreement concerning whether the attributes of Allah—exalted is He—can be described as eternal (qadīm)? Al-Ashʿarī describes them as such because they have no beginning to their existence, while ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd together with the early ones among our companions refrain from that, due to the impossibility of eternity (al-qidam)—which is a attribute—subsisting in attributes beyond itself, since an attribute does not subsist in another attribute, even though they said: there is no beginning to their existence. So this is as if a disagreement in expression. As for the originated (al-muḥdath), it is that which has a beginning to its existence, and it is said: that which has a first to its existence, and it is said: it is the originated in existence, and it is said: it is that which is subsequent by its existence to the eternal (al-azalī). All these expressions indicate a single meaning. The name 'eternal' (qadīm) may be applied to that which has a beginning to its existence, so it is said: this is an ancient building and an old man, intending precedence in existence over others with the condition of exaggeration. And this type is not intended in the discussion of whether the world is eternal or originated. And Allah is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage begins by noting a disagreement between al-Ashʿarī and the early Māturīdīs (represented by ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd al-Qalānisi and others) over whether God's attributes can be described as 'qadīm' (eternal). Al-Ashʿarī permits it because the attributes have no beginning to their existence. The Māturīdīs refrain, arguing that an attribute cannot subsist in another attribute, and since eternity (qidam) is itself an attribute, it cannot be attributed to other attributes. However, both sides agree that the attributes have no beginning; the disagreement is merely verbal. Next, the passage defines 'muḥdath' (originated): it is something with a beginning to its existence, or something that enters existence after non-existence. It then notes that 'qadīm' can colloquially describe something with a beginning but very old (like an 'ancient building' or 'old man'), but this usage is not meant when discussing the world's eternity or origination in theology. The passage ends with 'And Allah is the giver of success.'

Key Terms
قديم (qadīm)
صفة (ṣifa)
قدم (al-qidam)
أزلي (azalī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Māturīdī position implies they believe God's attributes have a beginning.
Correction: Both schools affirm the attributes have no beginning; the Māturīdīs simply avoid the term 'qadīm' because of a logical principle regarding attributes.

Checkpoint
Why do the early Māturīdīs refrain from describing God's attributes as 'qadīm'?
A) They believe attributes have a beginning.
B) Because an attribute cannot subsist in another attribute.
C) Because they follow al-Ashʿarī.
D) Because the term 'qadīm' is exclusively for God's essence.

Key Takeaway
The disagreement on describing attributes as qadīm is a verbal difference, as both schools affirm the attributes have no beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that a disagreement among scholars can be merely verbal. Think of a time you assumed a difference in belief was real. How might that assumption have affected your attitude towards others? Will you now reflect before judging, seeking to understand if the difference is truly substantive or only in expression?
?
If describing an attribute of Allah as 'qadīm' could lead to misunderstanding about the attribute subsisting in another attribute, are you careful with the words you use to describe Allah? Examine your own speech—do you ever use casual or careless terms about His attributes? Commit to learning precise, respectful language for discussing His attributes.
